|P - C|^2 = (x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2.
|P - A|^2 = (x_P - a_x)^2 + (y_P - a_y)^2.

Set equal:

(x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2 = (x_P - a_x)^2 + (y_P - a_y)^2.

Expand:

(x_P^2 + 2 r1 x_P + r1^2) + y_P^2 = (x_P^2 - 2 a_x x_P + a_x^2) + (y_P^2 - 2 a_y y_P + a_y^2).

Cancel x_P^2 + y_P^2:

2 r1 x_P + r1^2 = -2 a_x x_P + a_x^2 - 2 a_y y_P + a_y^2.

Bring terms: 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 a_x x_P - a_x^2 - a_y^2 + 2 a_y y_P = 0? Actually we have:

2 r1 x_P + r1^2 = -2 a_x x_P + a_x^2 - 2 a_y y_P + a_y^2.

Bring all to one side: 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 a_x x_P - a_x^2 + 2 a_y y_P - a_y^2 = 0? Wait we need to move terms: 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 - (-2 a_x x_P + a_x^2 - 2 a_y y_P + a_y^2) = 0. Actually we can rearrange:

2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 a_x x_P - a_x^2 + 2 a_y y_P - a_y^2 = 0? Let's compute:

Left side L = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2. Right side R = -2 a_x x_P + a_x^2 - 2 a_y y_P + a_y^2.

Set L - R = 0 => 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 a_x x_P - a_x^2 + 2 a_y y_P - a_y^2 = 0.

So we have:

2 x_P (r1 + a_x) + r1^2 - a_x^2 - a_y^2 + 2 a_y y_P = 0.

But we know a_x^2 + a_y^2 = r1^2 (since A lies on Ω). So a_x^2 + a_y^2 = r1^2. Then r1^2 - (a_x^2 + a_y^2) = 0. So the terms cancel! Indeed r1^2 - a_x^2 - a_y^2 = 0. So we get:

2 x_P (r1 + a_x) + 2 a_y y_P = 0. Divide by 2: x_P (r1 + a_x) + a_y y_P = 0.

Thus:

a_y y_P = - x_P (r1 + a_x). So y_P = - x_P (r1 + a_x) / a_y.

Now we also have expression for a_x and a_y in terms of d, r1, r2. a_x = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). a_y = sqrt(r1^2 - a_x^2). That is positive.

Now let's compute P.

Given A=(0,0), C=(1/2, p - r1), D=(1/2, q + r2). Let’s denote c = (1/2, c_y) with c_y = p - r1. d = (1/2, d_y) with d_y = q + r2.

We need to find P = (x,y) such that |P|^2 = |P - c|^2 = |P - d|^2.

From |P|^2 = |P - c|^2 => |P|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·c + |c|^2 => 2 P·c = |c|^2 => P·c = |c|^2 / 2. (dot product)

Similarly, P·d = |d|^2 / 2.

So P is a point whose dot products with c and d are half the squared magnitudes. This is a system of two linear equations in x,y (since dot product with c is linear). So we can solve.

Let c = (c_x, c_y) = (1/2, c_y). d = (1/2, d_y). Then:

P·c = x*(1/2) + y*c_y = (x/2) + y c_y = (|c|^2)/2.
P·d = x*(1/2) + y d_y = (x/2) + y d_y = (|d|^2)/2.

Subtract: y (c_y - d_y) = (|c|^2 - |d|^2)/2. Then we can solve.

We also have that c_y and d_y are related to p,q. We can compute r1 and r2 explicitly: r1 = sqrt(1/4 + p^2). r2 = sqrt(1/4 + q^2). So c_y = p - sqrt(1/4 + p^2). d_y = q + sqrt(1/4 + q^2). Note that p - sqrt(1/4 + p^2) is negative; but that's okay.

Now we can compute P. However there might be a simpler approach: Since A=0 is a vertex, the circumcenter P is the midpoint of the arc (or the intersection of perpendicular bisectors). There is a known formula: For triangle with vertices at 0, c, d, the circumcenter is (c * |d|^2 - d * |c|^2) / (c * conj(d) - d * conj(c))? Actually in complex numbers, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices a,b,c is (a*(|b|^2 - |c|^2) + ...) / something. But we can compute with coordinates.

d = (d_x, d_y) = (1/2, d_y).

Then |c|^2 = (1/2)^2 + c_y^2 = 1/4 + c_y^2.
|d|^2 = 1/4 + d_y^2.

The equations:

(1) (x/2) + y c_y = (1/4 + c_y^2)/2 = 1/8 + c_y^2/2.
(2) (x/2) + y d_y = (1/4 + d_y^2)/2 = 1/8 + d_y^2/2.

Subtract (2)-(1): y (d_y - c_y) = (d_y^2 - c_y^2)/2 = ( (d_y - c_y)(d_y + c_y) ) /2.

If d_y ≠ c_y (which is true, since d_y > c_y), then we can divide by (d_y - c_y): y = (d_y + c_y) / 2.

So y = (c_y + d_y)/2. That's nice.

Then plug back into (1) to find x.

From (1): x/2 + y c_y = 1/8 + c_y^2/2. So x/2 = 1/8 + c_y^2/2 - y c_y.

Substitute y = (c_y + d_y)/2. Compute y c_y = c_y*(c_y + d_y)/2 = (c_y^2 + c_y d_y)/2.

Then:

x/2 = 1/8 + c_y^2/2 - (c_y^2 + c_y d_y)/2 = 1/8 + (c_y^2/2) - (c_y^2/2) - (c_y d_y)/2 = 1/8 - (c_y d_y)/2.

So x/2 = 1/8 - (c_y d_y)/2 => x = 1/4 - c_y d_y.

Thus P = (x_P, y_P) = (1/4 - c_y d_y, (c_y + d_y)/2).

That's a clean expression.

Now we have c_y = p - r1, d_y = q + r2. So we can compute P in terms of p and q. Then AP is line through origin and P. So direction vector is (x_P, y_P). So AP is just the line with slope y_P / x_P if x_P ≠ 0.

This is a quadratic in t. Since t=0 (A) is a root, the other root t_E gives E. So we can compute t_E = (2 M·P) / (|P|^2) maybe? Let's derive.

We have |t P - M|^2 = r1^2. Expand: t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (M·P) + |M|^2 = r1^2. But |M|^2 = r1^2. So:

t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (M·P) + r1^2 = r1^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (M·P) = 0 → t ( t |P|^2 - 2 (M·P) ) = 0.

Thus t=0 (A) or t = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2. So E = (2 (M·P) / |P|^2) P.

Similarly, F = (2 (N·P) / |P|^2) P, provided that N·P is nonzero, and that the second intersection is distinct. So indeed E and F lie on OP.

y_P = (m + n + r2 - r1) / 2.

Now M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n). Then we can compute M·P and N·P.

N·P = (1/2) x_P + n y_P.

F = (2 (N·P) / |P|^2) P.

We have M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n). P = (x_P, y_P). Then we want H = (h, y_P) such that (H - M) · (N - P) = 0. That's one equation. Let's compute:

N - P = (1/2 - x_P, n - y_P).
H - M = (h - 1/2, y_P - m).

Dot: (h - 1/2)*(1/2 - x_P) + (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = 0.

Thus:

(h - 1/2)*(1/2 - x_P) = - (y_P - m)*(n - y_P).

So:

h = 1/2 + [ - (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ] / (1/2 - x_P).

That's similar. We can also use condition (H - N) · (M - P) = 0, which should give same result.

Indeed:

(1 - sin θ) / cos θ = cos θ / (1 + sin θ). (Provided cos θ ≠ 0, which is true because |θ| < π/2). So:

Let's try to compute using the rational parameterization. We'll denote:

θ, φ ∈ (-π/2, π/2). Then:

n = (1/2) tan φ.

Define also:

r1 = 1/(2 cos θ) → r1 - m = (1/2) * (1/cos θ - tan θ) = (1/2)*( (1 - sin θ)/cos θ ) = (1/2)*(cos θ/(1+ sin θ)). Good.

Similarly, r2 = 1/(2 cos φ) → n + r2 = (1/2)*(tan φ + sec φ) = (1/2)*( (sin φ + 1)/cos φ). So d_y = (1/2)*(1+ sin φ)/cos φ.

Now also note that (1 - sin θ) / cos θ = cos θ / (1 + sin θ). So c_y = - (1/2)*(cos θ/(1+ sin θ)). That's what we have.

Now compute y_P:

y_P = (1/4)*( (1+ sin φ)/cos φ - cos θ/(1+ sin θ) ). (3)

Now also compute x_P:

x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y = 1/4 - ( - (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ )? Actually c_y d_y = ( - (1/2)*(cos θ/(1+ sin θ)) ) * ( (1/2)*(1+ sin φ)/cos φ ) = - (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ). So x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y = 1/4 - [ - (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ) ] = 1/4 + (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ). So:

x_P = (1/4) * [ 1 + (cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ) ].

Now maybe we can simplify by expressing (1+ sin φ)/cos φ in terms of something like tan(φ/2) etc. Indeed, we have standard rational parameterizations: sin φ = 2t/(1+t^2), cos φ = (1 - t^2)/(1+t^2) with t = tan(φ/2). Then (1+ sin φ)/cos φ becomes something like (1 + 2t/(1+t^2)) / ((1 - t^2)/(1+t^2)) = ((1+t^2+2t)/(1+t^2)) * ((1+t^2)/(1 - t^2)) = ( (1+t)^2 )/(1 - t^2) = ( (1+t)^2 )/( (1-t)(1+t) ) = (1+t)/(1-t). That's a nice simplification. Indeed, if we set t = tan(φ/2), then (1+ sin φ)/cos φ = (1+t)/(1-t). Similarly, cos θ/(1+ sin θ) can be expressed using half-angle. Let's compute.

We have:

sin θ = 2u/(1+u^2), cos θ = (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2), where u = tan(θ/2). Then cos θ/(1+ sin θ) = ((1 - u^2)/(1+u^2)) / (1 + 2u/(1+u^2)) = ((1 - u^2)/(1+u^2)) / ((1+u^2+2u)/(1+u^2)) = (1 - u^2) / (1+u^2+2u) = (1 - u^2) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = (1 - u^2) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = (1 - u)(1+u) / (1+u)^2 = (1 - u)/(1+u).

Thus cos θ/(1+ sin θ) = (1 - u)/(1+u). That's nice. So we can simplify expressions.

Now also note that m = (1/2) tan θ. tan θ = 2u/(1 - u^2). So m = (1/2)*(2u/(1 - u^2)) = u/(1 - u^2). That's a rational expression. Similarly, n = v/(1 - v^2) where v = tan(φ/2). And r1 = 1/(2 cos θ) = 1/(2 * (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2)) = (1+u^2) / (2(1 - u^2)). That's also rational. So we have a rational parameterization in terms of u = tan(θ/2), v = tan(φ/2), with u,v ∈ ℝ, but for θ ∈ (-π/2, π/2), θ/2 ∈ (-π/4, π/4), so u ∈ (-1, 1). Similarly, v ∈ (-1, 1) because φ ∈ (-π/2, π/2). However we also have conditions like θ < φ and |θ| < |φ|. That translates to something about u and v. But that's okay.

Now we can compute P coordinates in terms of u and v, which might simplify a lot. Let's do that.

Let:

u = tan(θ/2) → θ ∈ (-π/2, π/2) → θ/2 ∈ (-π/4, π/4) → u ∈ (-1, 1). Similarly, v ∈ (-1, 1).

Then:

cos θ = (1 - u^2)/(1 + u^2). sin θ = 2u/(1 + u^2). tan θ = 2u/(1 - u^2). (1)

cos φ = (1 - v^2)/(1 + v^2). sin φ = 2v/(1 + v^2). tan φ = 2v/(1 - v^2). (2)

Now compute c_y = m - r1. But we have expression for c_y in terms of θ: c_y = - (1/2)*(cos θ)/(1 + sin θ). That's simple. Let's compute that using u.

cos θ / (1 + sin θ) = ( (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2) ) / ( 1 + 2u/(1+u^2) ) = ( (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2) ) / ( (1+u^2 + 2u)/(1+u^2) ) = (1 - u^2) / (1+u^2+2u) = (1 - u^2) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = ( (1-u)(1+u) ) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = (1-u)/(1+u).

So c_y = -1/2 * (1-u)/(1+u). Good.

Now compute d_y = n + r2. We have expression d_y = (1/2)*(1+ sin φ)/cos φ. Compute (1+ sin φ)/cos φ using v:

sin φ = 2v/(1+v^2), cos φ = (1 - v^2)/(1+v^2). Then:

1 + sin φ = (1+v^2 + 2v)/(1+v^2) = (1+v)^2/(1+v^2). So (1+ sin φ)/cos φ = ((1+v)^2/(1+v^2)) / ((1 - v^2)/(1+v^2)) = (1+v)^2 / (1 - v^2) = (1+v)^2 / ((1-v)(1+v)) = (1+v)/(1-v). So:

d_y = (1/2) * (1+v)/(1-v). That's nice.

Thus we have:

c_y = - (1/2) * (1 - u) / (1 + u). (1)
d_y = (1/2) * (1 + v) / (1 - v). (2)

Now also m = (1/2) tan θ = (1/2) * (2u/(1 - u^2)) = u/(1 - u^2). Similarly, n = v/(1 - v^2). These are rational.

Now we can compute P = (x_P, y_P) as before:

x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y.
y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2.

Let's compute c_y d_y:

c_y d_y = - (1/2)*(1-u)/(1+u) * (1/2)*(1+v)/(1-v) = - (1/4) * ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ).

Thus:

x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y = 1/4 + (1/4) * ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) = (1/4) * [ 1 + ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ].

Now compute y_P:

y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2 = ( - (1/2)*(1-u)/(1+u) + (1/2)*(1+v)/(1-v) ) / 2 = (1/2)*( - (1-u)/(1+u) + (1+v)/(1-v) ) / 2 = (1/4)*( - (1-u)/(1+u) + (1+v)/(1-v) ).

Simplify:

y_P = (1/4) * ( (1+v)/(1-v) - (1-u)/(1+u) ). That's neat.

Now we have v = P (vector). So v = (x_P, y_P). That's still messy but perhaps we can find α and β.

Let's compute M·P and N·P. M = (1/2, m) = (1/2, u/(1-u^2)). N = (1/2, n) = (1/2, v/(1-v^2)). That's rational. Then:

M·P = (1/2) x_P + m y_P = (1/2) x_P + (u/(1-u^2)) y_P.
N·P = (1/2) x_P + (v/(1-v^2)) y_P.

Now α = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2. That's a scalar. Since P is a vector, |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2.

Then E = α P, F = β P.

Now B = (1,0). So we have triangle BEF. The line L through H parallel to AP is L: H + t P. We need to prove L is tangent to circumcircle of BEF. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since MN is vertical, H lies on horizontal line y = y_P. So H = (h, y_P). We need to compute h. The expression for h is:

h = 1/2 - ( (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ) / (1/2 - x_P). That's from earlier. But now we have parameterization. Maybe we can compute that expression and simplify. Let's try.

We have:

m = u/(1-u^2). n = v/(1-v^2). y_P = (1/4)*( (1+v)/(1-v) - (1-u)/(1+u) ). Also x_P = (1/4)*( 1 + ((1-u)(1+v))/((1+u)(1-v)) ). That's messy but perhaps simplifies to something like (1/4)*(something). Let's compute x_P in terms of u,v:

x_P = (1/4) * ( 1 + ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ). Combine the fraction: Write 1 as ( (1+u)(1-v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ). Then:

x_P = (1/4) * ( ( (1+u)(1-v) + (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ).

Compute numerator:

(1+u)(1-v) + (1-u)(1+v) = (1 - v + u - uv) + (1 + v - u - uv) = (1 - v + u - uv) + (1 + v - u - uv) = 2 - 2uv. Because -v + v cancels, u - u cancels, -uv - uv = -2uv. So numerator = 2(1 - uv). Thus:

x_P = (1/4) * ( 2(1 - uv) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ) = (1/2) * ( (1 - uv) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ).

So:

x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.   (4)

That's a nice simplification! Good.

Now compute y_P similarly:

y_P = \frac{1}{4} \left( \frac{1+v}{1-v} - \frac{1-u}{1+u} \right).

Combine into a single fraction:

Let’s compute A = \frac{1+v}{1-v} - \frac{1-u}{1+u} = \frac{ (1+v)(1+u) - (1-u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)(1+u) }.

Compute numerator:

(1+v)(1+u) = (1+u+v+uv). (1-u)(1-v) = (1 - u - v + uv). Subtract: (1+u+v+uv) - (1 - u - v + uv) = 1+u+v+uv -1 + u + v - uv = 2u + 2v. So numerator = 2(u+v). Thus:

A = \frac{2(u+v)}{(1-v)(1+u)}.

Therefore:

y_P = \frac{1}{4} * A = \frac{1}{4} * \frac{2(u+v)}{(1-v)(1+u)} = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

So:

y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.   (5)

Great! So P = (x_P, y_P) with:

x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.
y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Now note that (1+u)(1-v) > 0 because u,v ∈ (-1,1) but (1+u) > 0, (1-v) > 0 (since v<1). Indeed, u ∈ (-1,1) → 1+u > 0; v ∈ (-1,1) → 1-v > 0. So denominator positive. So P coordinates are rational functions.

Now also m = u/(1-u^2). We can compute r1 - m expression we had. But maybe we also need (y_P - m) and (n - y_P). Let's compute those.

Compute (1+u)(1-v) = 1 - v + u - uv. Subtract 1 gives -v + u - uv. Then add uv gives -v + u - uv + uv = u - v. So numerator simplifies to u - v. Indeed:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{ u - v }{ (1+u)(1-v) } = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Great. So:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.   (6)

Combine terms over common denominator? That seems messy. However we might also compute |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2. That's also messy. But maybe α and β have simple forms. Let's try to compute α * P? \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)}. So we need to combine.

y_P - m = \frac{1}{1+u} \left( \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} \right) - \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{1}{1+u} \left( \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u} \right).

Now we can compute (y_P - m)*(n - y_P). That's product of two terms, each with factor 1/(1+u) and 1/(1-v). So product becomes:

(Δ + uv)^2 = u^2 + v^2 - 2uv + 2uv^2 - 2u^2 v + u^2 v^2.

Now (Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) = (v - u + uv)(u^2 + v^2). Expand: (v - u)(u^2 + v^2) + uv (u^2 + v^2). (v - u)(u^2 + v^2) = v(u^2+v^2) - u(u^2+v^2) = v u^2 + v^3 - u^3 - u v^2. So:

(Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) = (v u^2 + v^3 - u^3 - u v^2) + uv(u^2+v^2) = (v u^2 + v^3 - u^3 - u v^2) + u^3 v + u v^3.

Then u^2 v^2 appears.

Now N1 N2 = (Δ + uv)^2 - (Δ + uv)(u^2+v^2) + u^2 v^2.

Plug in and simplify. That seems messy. However perhaps we can notice that (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) simplifies nicely when we compute the ratio needed for h. Since h = 1/2 - ( (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ) / (1/2 - x_P). And we have (1/2 - x_P) = \frac{u - v}{2 D}. So:

h = 1/2 - \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ \frac{u - v}{2 D} } = 1/2 - \frac{2 D}{u - v} * (y_P - m)*(n - y_P). But careful: (1/2 - x_P) = (u - v) / (2 D). So dividing by that is multiplying by (2 D)/(u - v). So:

h = 1/2 - \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ (u - v) / (2 D) = 1/2 - \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) * 2 D }{ u - v }.

Thus h = 1/2 - \frac{2 D}{u - v} * (y_P - m)*(n - y_P). (But note that u - v could be zero? If u = v, then M = N? Actually u = tan(θ/2), v = tan(φ/2). If u = v, then θ = φ, so m = n, circles coincide (since same center and radius). The problem states they intersect at two distinct points, so they are not identical, so u ≠ v. So safe.

Now we have expressions for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) and D. Let's compute the product (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) in terms of u,v using the simplified expressions we derived. Simplify: 2 cancels with 4 gives 2 in denominator? Actually 2/4 = 1/2. So we have:

Thus:

- x + 2 x x_P = x_P^2 + (y - y_P)^2 - 1/4 - ((n - m)^2)/4.

y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 D}. m = \frac{u}{1-u^2}. n = \frac{v}{1-v^2}. D = (1+u)(1-v). So:

y_P - m = \frac{u+v}{2 D} - \frac{u}{1-u^2}. Similarly, n - y_P = \frac{v}{1-v^2} - \frac{u+v}{2 D}.

We need the product (y_P - m)*(n - y_P). Maybe we can find a simplification by noting that (y_P - m) and (n - y_P) are related to (1/2 - x_P) and something like (u - v). Let's attempt to compute y_P - m in a more structured way. Write m = \frac{u}{1-u^2}. Express 1-u^2 = (1-u)(1+u). So:

m = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)}.

Now y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. So we have denominators involving (1+u) and (1-v). So we can combine.

Similarly, n = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)}.

Now compute y_P - m:

y_P - m = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} - \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{1}{1+u} \left( \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u} \right). So we need B1 = \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u}.

We earlier computed B1 = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u) }. That seems messy. But perhaps we can simplify by expressing u and v in terms of tangents of half-angles. But we already have rational expressions. Maybe there is a more clever way to find h without computing the product explicitly. Since H is orthocenter, we also have that H = P + M + N - 2 O, where O is circumcenter. We can compute O as the intersection of perpendicular bisector of MN (horizontal) and perpendicular bisector of PM. That's a linear system. So we can solve for O's coordinates. Then compute h = x_P + 1 - 2 x_O. That might simplify because we can find x_O in terms of x_P and something. Let's try that.

We have M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n). The perpendicular bisector of MN is y = (m+n)/2. So O = (x_O, (m+n)/2). Now also |O - M|^2 = |O - P|^2. Write the equation.

Let y0 = (m+n)/2. Then O - M = (x_O - 1/2, y0 - m) = (x_O - 1/2, (n - m)/2). O - P = (x_O - x_P, y0 - y_P). Then:

(x_O - 1/2)^2 + ((n - m)/2)^2 = (x_O - x_P)^2 + (y0 - y_P)^2.   (A)

We can solve for x_O. Expand:

(x_O^2 - x_O + 1/4) + ((n - m)^2)/4 = (x_O^2 - 2 x_O x_P + x_P^2) + (y0 - y_P)^2.

Cancel x_O^2:

- x_O + 1/4 + ((n - m)^2)/4 = -2 x_O x_P + x_P^2 + (y0 - y_P)^2.

Bring terms with x_O to LHS: - x_O + 2 x_O x_P = x_P^2 + (y0 - y_P)^2 - 1/4 - ((n - m)^2)/4.

So:

x_O (2 x_P - 1) = x_P^2 + (y0 - y_P)^2 - 1/4 - ((n - m)^2)/4.

Thus:

x_O = \frac{ x_P^2 + (y0 - y_P)^2 - 1/4 - ((n - m)^2)/4 }{ 2 x_P - 1 }.   (B)

Now compute numerator. We have y0 = (m+n)/2. So y0 - y_P = (m+n)/2 - y_P.

Now (m+n)^2 - (n-m)^2 = 4 m n. So ((m+n)^2)/4 - ((n-m)^2)/4 = m n. That's a nice simplification. So:

x_P^2 + (y0 - y_P)^2 - 1/4 - ((n - m)^2)/4 = x_P^2 + ( (m+n)/2 - y_P )^2 - 1/4 - ((n - m)^2)/4.

But we can also expand ( (m+n)/2 - y_P )^2 = (m+n)^2/4 - (m+n) y_P + y_P^2. Then combine with - ((n - m)^2)/4. So:

x_P^2 + \frac{ (m+n)^2 - (n-m)^2 }{4} - (m+n) y_P + y_P^2 - \frac{1}{4} = x_P^2 + \frac{4 m n}{4} - (m+n) y_P + y_P^2 - \frac{1}{4} = x_P^2 + m n - (m+n) y_P + y_P^2 - \frac{1}{4}.

Thus:

x_O = \frac{ x_P^2 + y_P^2 + m n - (m+n) y_P - \frac{1}{4} }{ 2 x_P - 1 }.

That's what we had earlier. So that's correct. Now we need to compute h = x_P + 1 - 2 x_O. That's an expression. Let's compute using rational forms. Maybe many terms cancel.

We have:

2 x_P - 1 = \frac{v - u}{D} (since we computed 2 x_P - 1 = \frac{v - u}{D} = -\frac{u - v}{D}). That's okay.

Now we need to compute the numerator N_num = x_P^2 + y_P^2 + m n - (m+n) y_P - 1/4. We already have x_P^2 + y_P^2 expressed. So we need m n, (m+n) y_P. Then we can compute x_O. Then h.

y_P - m = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} - \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{1}{1+u} \left( \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u} \right). So define:

A = \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u}.

Now A can be simplified:

A = \frac{ (u+v)(1-u) - 2u(1-v) }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u) }.

Compute numerator:

N_A = (u+v)(1-u) - 2u(1-v) = (u+v) - (u+v)u - 2u + 2uv = (u+v) - u^2 - uv - 2u + 2uv = (u+v - 2u) + (-u^2) + (-uv + 2uv) = (v - u) - u^2 + uv.

So N_A = v - u - u^2 + uv.

Thus A = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u) }.

Now y_P - m = \frac{1}{1+u} * A = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u)(1+u) }.

Simplify denominator: (1-v)(1-u)(1+u) = (1-v)(1-u^2). So:

y_P - m = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u^2) }.

Similarly, compute n - y_P:

n - y_P = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)} - \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. Factor 1/(1-v) maybe:

y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} = \frac{1}{1-v} \cdot \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)}.

Thus:

Compute B = \frac{v}{1+v} - \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)}. Put over common denominator 2 (1+v)(1+u):

We have:

m = \frac{u}{1-u^2}
n = \frac{v}{1-v^2}

Also, r1 = \frac{1+u^2}{2(1-u^2)}. r2 = \frac{1+v^2}{2(1-v^2)}.

Now compute n - m:

n - m = \frac{v}{1-v^2} - \frac{u}{1-u^2}.

To combine, find common denominator: (1-v^2)(1-u^2). Then:

n - m = \frac{ v(1-u^2) - u(1-v^2) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Compute numerator:

v(1-u^2) - u(1-v^2) = v - v u^2 - u + u v^2 = (v - u) + uv^2 - u^2 v = (v - u) + uv(v - u) = (v - u)(1 + uv).

Thus:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.   (4)

Now compute r2 - r1:

r2 - r1 = \frac{1+v^2}{2(1-v^2)} - \frac{1+u^2}{2(1-u^2)} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1+v^2}{1-v^2} - \frac{1+u^2}{1-u^2} \right).

Combine the fractions inside: \frac{1+v^2}{1-v^2} - \frac{1+u^2}{1-u^2}. Let's compute common denominator (1-v^2)(1-u^2). Then:

\frac{ (1+v^2)(1-u^2) - (1+u^2)(1-v^2) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Compute numerator:

(1+v^2)(1-u^2) - (1+u^2)(1-v^2) = (1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2) - (1 - v^2 + u^2 - u^2 v^2) = (1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2) - 1 + v^2 - u^2 + u^2 v^2 = combine: 1 - 1 cancels, -u^2 - u^2 = -2u^2? Wait careful: (1+v^2)(1-u^2) = 1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2. (1+u^2)(1-v^2) = 1 - v^2 + u^2 - u^2 v^2. Subtract: (1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2) - (1 - v^2 + u^2 - u^2 v^2) = 1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2 - 1 + v^2 - u^2 + u^2 v^2 = (-u^2 - u^2) + (v^2 + v^2) + (-u^2 v^2 + u^2 v^2) = -2u^2 + 2v^2.

Thus:

\frac{1+v^2}{1-v^2} - \frac{1+u^2}{1-u^2} = \frac{2(v^2 - u^2)}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) } = \frac{2(v-u)(v+u)}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Therefore:

r2 - r1 = \frac{1}{2} * \frac{2(v-u)(v+u)}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) } = \frac{ (v-u)(v+u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.   (5)

Now note that (v-u) = -(u-v). So we have expressions for n - m and r2 - r1. Now we can compute (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2.

We have:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.
r2 - r1 = \frac{ (v - u)(v + u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Thus both have the same factor (v - u) / ((1-v^2)(1-u^2)). Let’s denote:

K = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Then:

n - m = K * (1 + uv).
r2 - r1 = K * (v + u).

Now compute:

(n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = K^2 [ (1 + uv)^2 - (v + u)^2 ].

Simplify (1 + uv)^2 - (v + u)^2:

(1 + uv)^2 = 1 + 2uv + u^2 v^2.
(v + u)^2 = u^2 + 2uv + v^2.

So difference = 1 + 2uv + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - 2uv - v^2 = 1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2.

Thus:

(n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = K^2 * (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2).

Now we have:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4} = \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now compute K^2:

K^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Now we also have (1/2 - x_P) = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. So (u - v) = -(v - u). But we'll keep.

Now h = 1/2 - \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P }.

Plug in:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 D}, where D = (1+u)(1-v). So 1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Now (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Thus:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} }{ \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} } = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v }.

Simplify: \frac{2}{4} = \frac{1}{2}. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ u - v }.

Now K^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } because square. So we can write (v - u)^2 = (u - v)^2.

Thus:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ u - v }.

Note that (u - v)^2 / (u - v) = (u - v). So we have:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{? Actually we have factor (1+u)(1-v) in numerator. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * (1+u)(1-v).

But we can also simplify (1-v^2) = (1-v)(1+v), and (1-u^2) = (1-u)(1+u). So:

(1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 = (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2.

Thus:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1+u)(1-v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 }.

Cancel one (1+u) and one (1-v) maybe? Let's simplify:

\frac{ (u - v) (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) } * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{(1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 }? Actually we have denominator (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2. Numerator (u - v) (1+u)(1-v). So we can cancel one (1-v) from denominator, leaving (1-v) in denominator. Also cancel one (1+u) from denominator, leaving (1+u) in denominator. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

But careful: after canceling, denominator becomes (1-v) * (1+v)^2 * (1-u)^2 * (1+u). Yes.

So h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Now this seems still messy. However note that we also have expressions for m and n, and we might compute the coordinates of E and F in terms of u,v. Then the circle (BEF) maybe simplifies and the tangency condition becomes something like (h - 1/2) is something that makes L tangent. But this is heavy.

Perhaps we can find a different parameterization that makes P simpler. The earlier param with u and v gave P in a simple rational form. That's good. Now perhaps we can also find that H has a simple expression in terms of u and v. Let's try to compute h directly using the formula h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). We already have an expression for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) in terms of K and (1+u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2). And we have (1/2 - x_P) expressed. Maybe the ratio simplifies to something like \frac{ (1+u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) (u - v) }{2 (1+u)(1-v) (something)}. Let's try to compute h more directly with the rational forms, but maybe we can find a pattern by computing M·P and N·P, then find α and β, then the circle (BEF) maybe has a simple equation, like (x - something)^2 + (y - something)^2 = something, and then L's distance is radius. Another thought: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, the line AP is the line through A and the circumcenter. The line through H parallel to AP is L. Actually the Simson line of a point on the circumcircle of a triangle is a line that passes through the feet of perpendiculars. If we take the point P (maybe P is on the circumcircle of BEF?) That could be interesting. Let's check if P lies on (BEF). P is the circumcenter of ACD. Not obviously related. But maybe we can prove that P, B, E, F are concyclic? Let's examine. E and F are on AP. Given the difficulty, perhaps we should attempt a synthetic approach. Let's try to understand the configuration in terms of known lemmas.

Let's compute H's coordinates with the rational expressions we have. Perhaps we can find that h is something like \frac{1}{2} - \frac{(1-u)(1+v)}{4 (1+u)(1-v)}? Not sure.

Let's compute using the simplified expressions for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) and 1/2 - x_P, but also express (n - m) and (r2 - r1) in terms of u and v. Then compute the ratio and simplify.

We have:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. (A)

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4}. We expressed:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.
r2 - r1 = \frac{ (v - u)(v + u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

So define:

Δ = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }. Then n - m = Δ (1+uv), r2 - r1 = Δ (v+u). Then:

(n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = Δ^2 [ (1+uv)^2 - (v+u)^2 ] = Δ^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2). So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ Δ^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now compute Δ^2:

Δ^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Thus:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now (1-v^2) = (1-v)(1+v), (1-u^2) = (1-u)(1+u). So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now (1/2 - x_P) = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. So:

\frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} }{ \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v }.

Simplify: \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ u - v } = u - v. So we have:

\frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * 2 (1+u)(1-v).

= \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * 2 (1+u)(1-v)?? Actually we have factor 2 (1+u)(1-v) in numerator. So:

= \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 }.

Simplify further: \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ 1 }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Thus:

\frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ 1 }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Now h = 1/2 - that expression. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

That's what we had. So h is something like 1/2 minus a fraction.

Now the line L through H parallel to AP (direction P) is L: H + t P. Since P is not a unit vector. So L's direction is (x_P, y_P). So L has slope y_P / x_P if x_P != 0. Let's try to compute the circle (BEF) using the fact that E and F are multiples of P. Then the circle passing through B and those points can be expressed as the set of points X such that (X - B) · v? Actually we can find the equation of the circle in terms of P and B. Since E = α P and F = β P, we can param. The circle (BEF) is the circumcircle of triangle B, α P, β P. Its center O_BEF is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting α P and β P is the line perpendicular to P passing through ((α+β)/2) P. So O_BEF lies on that line. Also, O_BEF is equidistant to B and α P. So O_BEF is the center.

We can find O_BEF coordinates by solving the system. Let’s denote v = P = (a,b). Then α P = (α a, α b). β P = (β a, β b). B = (1,0). We need to find O = (x,y) such that:

|O - B|^2 = |O - α P|^2 = |O - β P|^2.

From |O - B|^2 = |O - α P|^2:

(x - 1)^2 + y^2 = (x - α a)^2 + (y - α b)^2.

Expand:

x^2 - 2x + 1 + y^2 = x^2 - 2α a x + α^2 a^2 + y^2 - 2α b y + α^2 b^2.

Cancel x^2 + y^2:

-2x + 1 = -2α a x + α^2 (a^2 + b^2) - 2α b y.

Bring terms:

-2x + 1 + 2α a x + 2α b y = α^2 |v|^2.

So:

2α (a x + b y) - 2x + 1 = α^2 |v|^2.   (1)

Similarly, from |O - B|^2 = |O - β P|^2:

-2x + 1 = -2β a x + β^2 |v|^2 - 2β b y. => 2β (a x + b y) - 2x + 1 = β^2 |v|^2.   (2)

Now also from |O - α P|^2 = |O - β P|^2:

(x - α a)^2 + (y - α b)^2 = (x - β a)^2 + (y - β b)^2. This simplifies to:

-2α a x - 2α b y + α^2|v|^2 = -2β a x - 2β b y + β^2|v|^2. => 2(β - α) (a x + b y) = (β^2 - α^2)|v|^2. That's an equation.

But perhaps we can find O explicitly. Since we know that the perpendicular bisector of α P and β P is the line: (x,y) = ((α+β)/2) v + s * v_perp. So O lies on that line. Also, O must satisfy the equation with B. So we can param O = ((α+β)/2) v + t * v_perp, where v_perp is a vector perpendicular to v. Then plug into |O - B|^2 = |O - α P|^2. That might yield t. That seems plausible. However the expressions might become messy.

I think the analytic approach with u,v is doable, but we need to compute O and R and then check the tangency condition. However the expressions might simplify nicely because many terms are rational and the cross product might become something like (something) * (u - v) etc. Let's try to compute step by step.

First, define u,v ∈ (-1,1). Let’s denote:

D = (1+u)(1-v). Then:

x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2D}
y_P = \frac{u+v}{2D}

Also, m = \frac{u}{1-u^2} = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)}. n = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)}.

We'll need M·P and N·P. Compute M·P:

M·P = \frac12 x_P + m y_P.

Compute \frac12 x_P = \frac12 * \frac{1-uv}{2D} = \frac{1-uv}{4D}.

m y_P = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} * \frac{u+v}{2D} = \frac{u(u+v)}{2D (1-u)(1+u)}.

Simplify denominator: (1-u)(1+u) = 1-u^2. So m y_P = \frac{u(u+v)}{2D (1-u^2)}.

Similarly, N·P = \frac12 x_P + n y_P = \frac{1-uv}{4D} + \frac{v(v+u)}{2D (1-v^2)}? Actually n = v/(1-v^2), so n y_P = \frac{v}{1-v^2} * \frac{u+v}{2D} = \frac{v(u+v)}{2D (1-v^2)}.

Now we also have |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2 = \frac{ (1-uv)^2 + (u+v)^2 }{4 D^2} = \frac{ 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2 }{4 D^2} as derived. Let's denote S = 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2.

Now α = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2 = \frac{2 (M·P)}{|P|^2}. So E = α P. Similarly for F.

Now we need to compute H. H = (h, y_P) with h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). But we also have an expression for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) in terms of n,m,r1,r2. That gave (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4}. We have formulas for n - m and r2 - r1. That seems manageable. However we might also compute h directly from the linear system of altitudes: (H - M) · (N - P) = 0, and H's y = y_P. That gave h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). We'll compute that.

Let’s compute (y_P - m) and (n - y_P) in terms of u,v, perhaps simpler than the (n - m) expression. We already have:

y_P - m = \frac{n - m + r2 - r1}{2}? Actually earlier we derived:

y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2 = (m - r1 + n + r2)/2 = \frac{m + n + r2 - r1}{2}. So:

y_P - m = \frac{m + n + r2 - r1}{2} - m = \frac{-m + n + r2 - r1}{2} = \frac{n - m + r2 - r1}{2}. (1)

n - y_P = n - \frac{m + n + r2 - r1}{2} = \frac{2n - m - n - r2 + r1}{2} = \frac{n - m - r2 + r1}{2}. (2)

Thus:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m + r2 - r1) (n - m - r2 + r1) }{4} = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4}. That's correct.

Now we have expressions for n - m and r2 - r1. We'll compute those.

We have:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

r2 - r1 = \frac{ (v - u)(v + u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

So both have factor (v - u) / ((1-v^2)(1-u^2)). Let’s denote:

F = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Then n - m = F (1+uv), r2 - r1 = F (v+u). Then (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = F^2 [ (1+uv)^2 - (u+v)^2 ] = F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2). So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now compute F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Also, 1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 D} = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. Since u - v = -(v - u), but squared is same. So we have:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Now compute the ratio:

R = \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} }{ \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} } = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v } = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v }.

Now F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

So \frac{ F^2 }{ u - v } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (u - v) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Thus:

R = \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * (1+u)(1-v).

Now note that (1-v^2) = (1-v)(1+v). So (1-v^2)^2 = (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2. Then:

R = \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * (1+u)(1-v).

Simplify: \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ 1 }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Thus:

R = \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Now h = 1/2 - R. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) (u - v) }{ 2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

This is messy. But maybe we can simplify further by noting that (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) = (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) - 2u^2 v^2? Actually (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = 1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2. That's 1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2. So our expression is 1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2 = (1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2). That's exactly (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2). Wait compute (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = 1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2. Yes! That's exactly our numerator. So:

1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2 = (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2). Good catch! That simplifies a lot. So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ F^2 (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) }{4}.

Now F = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }. So F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }. Then:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) }{4} = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{4 (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Thus:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{4 (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

That's much simpler! Let's double-check: (v - u)^2 / ( (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 ) * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = (v - u)^2 / ( (1-v^2)(1-u^2) ) * (1 / ( (1-v^2)(1-u^2) ))? Wait careful:

We have F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Then (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{1}{4} * F^2 * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = \frac{1}{4} * \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{4} * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) }? Actually we have (1-v^2)^2 in denominator, and multiply by (1 - v^2) gives (1-v^2) in denominator after canceling one factor: (1-v^2)^2 / (1 - v^2) = (1-v^2). So we get:

\frac{ (v - u)^2 }{4} * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) }? Let's compute step by step:

F^2 * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) }? Actually we have:

(1-v^2)^2 in denominator, times (1 - v^2) in numerator gives (1-v^2) in denominator. Similarly for (1-u^2). So:

F^2 * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) }? No, we have:

\frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * (1 - v^2)(1 - u^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) }? That would be \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * (1 - v^2)(1 - u^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2) (1-u^2) }? That's not correct because we have (1-v^2)^2 in denominator, multiplying by (1 - v^2) yields (1-v^2) in denominator, but we have (1-v^2) in numerator, so one factor cancels, leaving (1-v^2) in denominator. So:

Yes! Because \frac{ (1 - v^2)(1 - u^2) }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{1}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

That's even simpler! Let's verify: we had (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} = \frac{ F^2 (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) }{4}. Then substituting F^2 * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }. So indeed:

Put over common denominator 4 D (1-u^2). Then:

M·P = \frac{ (1-uv)(1-u^2) + 2 u(u+v) }{4 D (1-u^2)}.

N_M = 1 - uv - u^2 + u^3 v + 2u(u+v) = 1 - uv - u^2 + u^3 v + 2u^2 + 2uv = 1 + (-uv + 2uv) + (-u^2 + 2u^2) + u^3 v = 1 + uv + u^2 + u^3 v.

So N_M = 1 + u^2 + uv + u^3 v.

Thus:

M·P = \frac{ 1 + u^2 + uv + u^3 v }{4 D (1-u^2)}.

So N_N = 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3.

Thus:

N·P = \frac{ 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3 }{4 D (1-v^2)}.

Now |P|^2 = \frac{ 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2 }{4 D^2}. So:

Because 4 D^2 / (4 D) = D. So:

α = 2 D * \frac{ 1 + u^2 + uv + u^3 v }{ (1-u^2) T }.

Similarly, β = 2 (N·P) / |P|^2 = 2 * \frac{ 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3 }{4 D (1-v^2)} * \frac{4 D^2}{ T } = 2 D * \frac{ 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3 }{ (1-v^2) T }.

Now note that T = 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2. That's symmetric.

Let v = P. Then we have three points: B, αv, βv. The circle passing through them can be described by the equation:

|X - O|^2 = R^2, O = something.

Alternatively, we can find O by solving the system:

(O - B)·(O - B) = O - αv)·(O - αv) = O - βv)·(O - βv).

From O - αv and O - βv, we get:

|O - αv|^2 = |O - βv|^2 → (O·O - 2α O·v + α^2|v|^2) = (O·O - 2β O·v + β^2|v|^2) → -2α O·v + α^2|v|^2 = -2β O·v + β^2|v|^2 → 2(β - α) O·v = (β^2 - α^2)|v|^2 = (β - α)(β + α)|v|^2. If β ≠ α, divide by (β - α): 2 O·v = (α + β)|v|^2. So:

O·v = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2.   (1)

Thus O lies on the line (the set of points whose dot product with v is constant). That's a line perpendicular to v (since v is fixed). Actually the set {X | X·v = c} is a line (hyperplane) perpendicular to v. So O is on that line. Also, from |O - B|^2 = |O - αv|^2 we get:

|O - B|^2 = |O - αv|^2 → (O·O - 2 O·B + |B|^2) = (O·O - 2α O·v + α^2|v|^2). Cancel O·O:

-2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2α O·v + α^2|v|^2. So:

2 O·v - 2 O·B = α^2|v|^2 - |B|^2. Or:

2 O·(v - B) = α^2|v|^2 - |B|^2.   (2)

Similarly, using β would give another equation, but they should be consistent given (1). We can use (1) to express O·v in terms of α,β,|v|^2. Then plug into (2) to get an equation involving O·B. That's a linear equation in O (since O·B is dot product). So we have two linear equations: O·v = constant, and O·(v - B) = constant. That's two equations; they determine O uniquely up to? Actually O is a 2D vector. The equations O·v = c1 and O·(v - B) = c2 are two linear equations, which can be solved for O. So we can find O explicitly. That seems promising!

We have:

- v = P = (a,b).
- B = (1,0).
- |v|^2 = a^2 + b^2.
- α and β are scalars.

Then from (1): O·v = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2. Let’s denote C1 = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2.

From (2): 2 O·(v - B) = α^2 |v|^2 - |B|^2. Since |B|^2 = 1. So:

O·(v - B) = \frac{ α^2 |v|^2 - 1 }{2}.

Now note that v - B is a vector. So we have two linear equations. That's enough to solve for O. Then we can compute R = |O - B|. But perhaps we can also find that O lies on a line that is the reflection of something, and then L is tangent because O is on the circle with diameter something? Let's compute O explicitly.

Let’s denote:

v = (a,b). Then O·v = a O_x + b O_y = C1. Also, O·(v - B) = O·(v - (1,0)) = a O_x + b O_y - O_x = (a-1) O_x + b O_y = C2.

So we have:

(1) a O_x + b O_y = C1.
(2) (a-1) O_x + b O_y = C2.

Subtract (1)-(2): (a - (a-1)) O_x = (1) O_x = C1 - C2. So O_x = C1 - C2. Then O_y can be obtained from (1): b O_y = C1 - a O_x → O_y = (C1 - a O_x) / b, provided b ≠ 0. If b = 0, then P is horizontal, and AP is horizontal. That would be a special case where AP is horizontal. In that case, the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal, and we need to prove tangency. That case can be handled separately. So we can assume b ≠ 0 for generic configuration. Also, if b = 0, then P is horizontal, so AP is horizontal. Then L is horizontal. We can check if that case is allowed by the problem? It might be, if the configuration leads to P horizontal. But we can treat it as a separate case and prove that the statement holds (maybe by continuity or by direct computation). So we can focus on b ≠ 0.

Now O_x = C1 - C2. That's simple. So the center O is determined by O_x and O_y.

Now we need to show that the line L: H + t v is tangent to this circle. The distance from O to L is |det(O - H, v)| / |v|. Let's compute O - H. H = (h, y_P). So O - H = (O_x - h, O_y - y_P). Then det(O - H, v) = (O_x - h) b - (O_y - y_P) a. Since det((x,y),(a,b)) = x b - y a.

Thus we need to prove:

| (O_x - h) b - (O_y - y_P) a | / |v| = |O - B|.   (T)

Now we have expressions for O_x, O_y, h, y_P, a, b. Also, α and β are expressed in terms of u,v. So this becomes an equation in u,v. Maybe it simplifies.

Let's compute O_x and O_y in terms of α,β, a,b. We have:

C1 = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2.
C2 = \frac{α^2 |v|^2 - 1}{2}.

Then O_x = C1 - C2 = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2 - \frac{α^2 |v|^2 - 1}{2} = \frac{ (α + β) |v|^2 - α^2 |v|^2 + 1 }{2} = \frac{ (α + β - α^2) |v|^2 + 1 }{2}.

Similarly, we can compute O_y from a O_x + b O_y = C1. But maybe we can also find O_y by solving the linear system. Since we already have O_x, then O_y = (C1 - a O_x) / b.

Now also, from the condition that O is equidistant to B and αv, we could also find O_x in terms of α and β maybe simpler using the formula for the circumcenter of triangle with two points on a line. There is a formula: The center of the circle through points (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3) can be expressed. But maybe we can compute O_x and O_y directly from α,β, a,b using vector algebra. Let's do that.

We have three points: B = (1,0), A1 = α v, A2 = β v. Since A1 and A2 are collinear with origin, we can find the circle's equation by noting that the circle's chord A1A2 is along v. The perpendicular bisector of A1A2 is a line L1: (X - ((α+β)/2 v)) · v = 0. So L1 consists of points X such that (X·v) = ((α+β)/2) |v|^2. So O must satisfy O·v = ((α+β)/2) |v|^2. That's our C1. So O·v = C1. Good.

Also, O must be equidistant to B and A1. So |O - B|^2 = |O - α v|^2 → O·O - 2 O·B + 1 = O·O - 2α O·v + α^2 |v|^2 → -2 O·B + 1 = -2α O·v + α^2 |v|^2. So 2 O·v - 2 O·B = α^2 |v|^2 - 1. That's our C2 equation: O·(v - B) = (α^2 |v|^2 - 1)/2.

Now we can solve for O. Write O = (x,y). Then:

a x + b y = C1.   (1)
(a-1) x + b y = C2.   (2)

Subtract: (a - (a-1)) x = (1) x = C1 - C2. So O_x = C1 - C2. Then from (1): b y = C1 - a O_x. So O_y = (C1 - a O_x) / b. That's explicit.

Now we have O_x = C1 - C2. And C2 = (α^2 |v|^2 - 1)/2. So O_x = (α+β)/2 |v|^2 - (α^2 |v|^2 - 1)/2 = ( (α+β - α^2) |v|^2 + 1 ) / 2.

Now we also have H = (h, y_P). So O - H = (O_x - h, O_y - y_P). Then det(O - H, v) = (O_x - h) b - (O_y - y_P) a.

We need to show that |det(O - H, v)| / |v| = |O - B|. That's one equation.

Maybe we can find that O is actually the midpoint of something like the segment connecting B and the orthocenter H? No.

Now we have parameters p and q. Also, we have that circles intersect at A and B. The condition for intersection is that d = |p - q| < r1 + r2 and > |r1 - r2|. Now let's compute P.

Given A=(0,0), C=(1/2, p - r1), D=(1/2, q + r2). Let’s denote c = (1/2, c_y) with c_y = p - r1. d = (1/2, d_y) with d_y = q + r2.

We need to find P = (x,y) such that |P|^2 = |P - c|^2 = |P - d|^2.

From |P|^2 = |P - c|^2 => |P|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·c + |c|^2 => 2 P·c = |c|^2 => P·c = |c|^2 / 2. (dot product)

Similarly, P·d = |d|^2 / 2.

So P is a point whose dot products with c and d are half the squared magnitudes. This is a system of two linear equations in x,y (since dot product with c is linear). So we can solve.

Let c = (c_x, c_y) = (1/2, c_y). d = (1/2, d_y). Then:

P·c = x*(1/2) + y*c_y = (x/2) + y c_y = (|c|^2)/2.
P·d = x*(1/2) + y d_y = (x/2) + y d_y = (|d|^2)/2.

Subtract: y (c_y - d_y) = (|c|^2 - |d|^2)/2. Then we can solve.

We also have that c_y and d_y are related to p,q. We can compute r1 and r2 explicitly: r1 = sqrt(1/4 + p^2). r2 = sqrt(1/4 + q^2). So c_y = p - sqrt(1/4 + p^2). d_y = q + sqrt(1/4 + q^2). Note that p - sqrt(1/4 + p^2) is negative; but that's okay.

Now we can compute P. However there might be a simpler approach: Since A=0 is a vertex, the circumcenter P is the midpoint of the arc (or the intersection of perpendicular bisectors). There is a known formula: For triangle with vertices at 0, c, d, the circumcenter is (c * |d|^2 - d * |c|^2) / (c * conj(d) - d * conj(c))? Actually in complex numbers, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices a,b,c is (a*(|b|^2 - |c|^2) + ...) / something. But we can compute with coordinates.

Let's do coordinates.

Let’s denote:

c = (c_x, c_y) = (1/2, c_y).
d = (d_x, d_y) = (1/2, d_y).

Then |c|^2 = (1/2)^2 + c_y^2 = 1/4 + c_y^2.
|d|^2 = 1/4 + d_y^2.

The equations:

(1) (x/2) + y c_y = (1/4 + c_y^2)/2 = 1/8 + c_y^2/2.
(2) (x/2) + y d_y = (1/4 + d_y^2)/2 = 1/8 + d_y^2/2.

Subtract (2)-(1): y (d_y - c_y) = (d_y^2 - c_y^2)/2 = ( (d_y - c_y)(d_y + c_y) ) /2.

If d_y ≠ c_y (which is true, since d_y > c_y), then we can divide by (d_y - c_y): y = (d_y + c_y) / 2.

So y = (c_y + d_y)/2. That's nice.

Then plug back into (1) to find x.

From (1): x/2 + y c_y = 1/8 + c_y^2/2. So x/2 = 1/8 + c_y^2/2 - y c_y.

Substitute y = (c_y + d_y)/2. Compute y c_y = c_y*(c_y + d_y)/2 = (c_y^2 + c_y d_y)/2.

Then:

x/2 = 1/8 + c_y^2/2 - (c_y^2 + c_y d_y)/2 = 1/8 + (c_y^2/2) - (c_y^2/2) - (c_y d_y)/2 = 1/8 - (c_y d_y)/2.

So x/2 = 1/8 - (c_y d_y)/2 => x = 1/4 - c_y d_y.

Thus P = (x_P, y_P) = (1/4 - c_y d_y, (c_y + d_y)/2).

That's a clean expression.

Now we have c_y = p - r1, d_y = q + r2. So we can compute P in terms of p and q. Then AP is line through origin and P. So direction vector is (x_P, y_P). So AP is just the line with slope y_P / x_P if x_P ≠ 0.

Now we also need E and F. E is second intersection of line OP with Ω. Since A=0 is on Ω, and line OP passes through origin, we can param: point = t P, t ∈ ℝ. Then we want t such that |t P - M| = r1. This is a quadratic in t. Since t=0 (A) is a root, the other root t_E gives E. So we can compute t_E = (2 M·P) / (|P|^2) maybe? Let's derive.

We have |t P - M|^2 = r1^2. Expand: t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (M·P) + |M|^2 = r1^2. But |M|^2 = r1^2. So:

t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (M·P) + r1^2 = r1^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (M·P) = 0 → t ( t |P|^2 - 2 (M·P) ) = 0.

Thus t=0 (A) or t = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2. So E = (2 (M·P) / |P|^2) P.

Similarly, F = (2 (N·P) / |P|^2) P, provided that N·P is nonzero, and that the second intersection is distinct. So indeed E and F lie on OP.

Now we can compute P, E, F, H, L, then check tangency. That seems plausible.

Let's attempt to compute P in terms of m,n.

First compute r1 = sqrt(1/4 + m^2), r2 = sqrt(1/4 + n^2). Then:

c_y = m - r1.
d_y = n + r2.

Now P = (x_P, y_P) = (1/4 - c_y d_y, (c_y + d_y)/2). So we need c_y d_y and c_y + d_y. Let's compute them.

c_y d_y = (m - r1)(n + r2) = m n + m r2 - n r1 - r1 r2.
c_y + d_y = m + n + r2 - r1.

Thus:

x_P = 1/4 - (m n + m r2 - n r1 - r1 r2) = 1/4 - m n - m r2 + n r1 + r1 r2.
y_P = (m + n + r2 - r1) / 2.

Now M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n). Then we can compute M·P and N·P.

M·P = (1/2) x_P + m y_P.
N·P = (1/2) x_P + n y_P.

Then E = (2 (M·P) / |P|^2) P.
F = (2 (N·P) / |P|^2) P.

Now B = (1,0). So triangle BEF.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since MN is vertical, H has same y-coordinate as P (horizontal altitude). So H = (x_H, y_P). And we derived condition:

(x_H - 1/2)*(1/2 - x_P) + (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = 0 (since N - P = (1/2 - x_P, n - y_P)). So:

x_H = 1/2 - ( (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ) / (1/2 - x_P) . That's messy.

But maybe we can find a nicer expression for H using vector approach: For triangle PMN, the orthocenter H is given by H = P + M + N - 2 O, where O is circumcenter. But we can also use the fact that the altitude from P is horizontal. So H lies on horizontal line y = y_P. So H = (h, y_P). Then we can find h from the condition that MH ⟂ PN, or NH ⟂ PM. That seems simpler. Let's compute.

We have M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n). P = (x_P, y_P). Then we want H = (h, y_P) such that (H - M) · (N - P) = 0. That's one equation. Let's compute:

N - P = (1/2 - x_P, n - y_P).
H - M = (h - 1/2, y_P - m).

Dot: (h - 1/2)*(1/2 - x_P) + (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = 0.

Thus:

(h - 1/2)*(1/2 - x_P) = - (y_P - m)*(n - y_P).

So:

h = 1/2 + [ - (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ] / (1/2 - x_P).

That's similar. We can also use condition (H - N) · (M - P) = 0, which should give same result.

Now the line L through H parallel to AP (i.e., parallel to OP) has direction vector P. So param: L = H + λ P, λ ∈ ℝ. We need to prove that L is tangent to circumcircle of BEF.

Now the circumcircle of BEF can be expressed as the set of points X satisfying some equation. Since E and F are collinear with A and P, and B is off that line, the circle (BEF) is not degenerate. That's a classic technique: If a line is tangent to a circle, then it is the polar of the point of tangency. But we don't know the point of tangency. Since tan is increasing on (-π/2, π/2), this is equivalent to θ < φ. So we need θ < φ. That's plausible. Also r1 < r2 implies cos θ > cos φ, which is equivalent to |θ| < |φ|, but not necessarily θ < φ. For example, if θ = -30°, φ = 30°, then θ < φ, but |θ| = |φ|, then r1 = r2. So need strictly less. If θ = -20°, φ = 30°, then θ < φ, |θ| < |φ|, r1 < r2. That works. If θ = 10°, φ = 20°, then θ < φ, r1 < r2. So indeed, if we assume that m < n and r1 < r2, then we have θ < φ and |θ| < |φ|. But it's possible that m < n but |θ| > |φ|? Since tan is increasing, if m < n, then θ < φ. But r1 < r2 implies cos θ > cos φ, which is equivalent to |θ| < |φ|. So for both to hold, we need that the larger angle (φ) has larger absolute value. That is consistent. So we have θ < φ and |θ| < |φ|. That implies that θ and φ could be both positive, or θ negative and φ positive. But if θ negative and φ positive, then |θ| < φ? Actually if θ = -20°, φ = 30°, then |θ| = 20° < 30° = |φ|, so okay. Also θ < φ holds. So that's allowed. So we can set up parameters θ and φ such that m = (1/2) tan θ, n = (1/2) tan φ, with θ < φ, and also r1 = 1/(2 cos θ), r2 = 1/(2 cos φ). The condition that circles intersect (which is automatically satisfied because they share A and B) doesn't impose any extra constraints beyond distinct centers and the fact that A and B are common points. But we should check that with this parameterization, the point B (which is (1,0) after scaling) is indeed on both circles. That's enforced by the definition of M,N with coordinates (1/2,m) and (1/2,n). So we are good.

Now we have a nice rational parameterization. Let's adopt:

A = (0,0), B = (1,0). (After scaling AB = 1). Then M = (1/2, m) with m = (1/2) tan θ. N = (1/2, n) with n = (1/2) tan φ.

We also have r1 = 1/(2 cos θ), r2 = 1/(2 cos φ). The conditions: θ, φ ∈ (-π/2, π/2), and we also have that m < n and r1 < r2. As derived, m < n iff θ < φ. And r1 < r2 iff cos θ > cos φ, which is equivalent to |θ| < |φ|. Since both cos positive, this is a condition on absolute values. So we need θ < φ and |θ| < |φ|. That implies that either both are positive with θ < φ, or θ is negative and φ is positive with |θ| < φ. In both cases, we have tan θ < tan φ and |θ| < |φ|. So it's consistent.

Now we can compute P = circumcenter of ACD. We have A=(0,0), C=(1/2, m - r1), D=(1/2, n + r2). Let's compute m - r1 and n + r2 in terms of θ, φ.

m - r1 = (1/2) tan θ - 1/(2 cos θ) = (1/2) * (tan θ - sec θ) = (1/2) * ( (sin θ / cos θ) - 1/cos θ ) = (1/2) * ( (sin θ - 1) / cos θ ). That's not super nice, but we can also compute r1 - m = 1/(2 cos θ) - (1/2) tan θ = (1/2) * (1/cos θ - sin θ / cos θ) = (1/2) * ((1 - sin θ) / cos θ). That's simpler. And then c_y = m - r1 = - (r1 - m). So c_y = - (1/2) * ((1 - sin θ) / cos θ). That's okay.

Similarly, n = (1/2) tan φ, r2 = 1/(2 cos φ). Then n + r2 = (1/2) tan φ + 1/(2 cos φ) = (1/2) * (tan φ + sec φ) = (1/2) * ( (sin φ / cos φ) + 1/cos φ) = (1/2) * ( (sin φ + 1) / cos φ). So d_y = n + r2 = (1/2) * ( (1 + sin φ) / cos φ). That's positive.

Now c_y = - (1/2) * ((1 - sin θ) / cos θ). Let's denote:

There is identity: (1 - sin θ) / cos θ = (cos θ) / (1 + sin θ). Because (1 - sin θ)(1 + sin θ) = 1 - sin^2 θ = cos^2 θ. So (1 - sin θ) / cos θ = cos θ / (1 + sin θ). That's a nicer expression. Indeed:

(1 - sin θ) / cos θ = cos θ / (1 + sin θ). (Provided cos θ ≠ 0, which is true because |θ| < π/2). So:

Let's try to compute using the rational parameterization. We'll denote:

Similarly, r2 = 1/(2 cos φ) → n + r2 = (1/2)*(tan φ + sec φ) = (1/2)*( (sin φ + 1)/cos φ). So d_y = (1/2)*(1+ sin φ)/cos φ.

Now also note that (1 - sin θ) / cos θ = cos θ / (1 + sin θ). So c_y = - (1/2)*(cos θ/(1+ sin θ)). That's what we have.

Now compute y_P:

y_P = (1/4)*( (1+ sin φ)/cos φ - cos θ/(1+ sin θ) ). (3)

Now also compute x_P:

x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y = 1/4 - ( - (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ )? Actually c_y d_y = ( - (1/2)*(cos θ/(1+ sin θ)) ) * ( (1/2)*(1+ sin φ)/cos φ ) = - (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ). So x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y = 1/4 - [ - (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ) ] = 1/4 + (1/4)*(cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ). So:

x_P = (1/4) * [ 1 + (cos θ (1+ sin φ))/( (1+ sin θ) cos φ ) ].

Now maybe we can simplify by expressing (1+ sin φ)/cos φ in terms of something like tan(φ/2) etc. Indeed, we have standard rational parameterizations: sin φ = 2t/(1+t^2), cos φ = (1 - t^2)/(1+t^2) with t = tan(φ/2). Then (1+ sin φ)/cos φ becomes something like (1 + 2t/(1+t^2)) / ((1 - t^2)/(1+t^2)) = ((1+t^2+2t)/(1+t^2)) * ((1+t^2)/(1 - t^2)) = ( (1+t)^2 )/(1 - t^2) = ( (1+t)^2 )/( (1-t)(1+t) ) = (1+t)/(1-t). That's a nice simplification. Indeed, if we set t = tan(φ/2), then (1+ sin φ)/cos φ = (1+t)/(1-t). Similarly, cos θ/(1+ sin θ) can be expressed using half-angle. Let's compute.

We have:

sin θ = 2u/(1+u^2), cos θ = (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2), where u = tan(θ/2). Then cos θ/(1+ sin θ) = ((1 - u^2)/(1+u^2)) / (1 + 2u/(1+u^2)) = ((1 - u^2)/(1+u^2)) / ((1+u^2+2u)/(1+u^2)) = (1 - u^2) / (1+u^2+2u) = (1 - u^2) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = (1 - u^2) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = (1 - u)(1+u) / (1+u)^2 = (1 - u)/(1+u).

Thus cos θ/(1+ sin θ) = (1 - u)/(1+u). That's nice. So we can simplify expressions.

Now also note that m = (1/2) tan θ. tan θ = 2u/(1 - u^2). So m = (1/2)*(2u/(1 - u^2)) = u/(1 - u^2). That's a rational expression. Similarly, n = v/(1 - v^2) where v = tan(φ/2). And r1 = 1/(2 cos θ) = 1/(2 * (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2)) = (1+u^2) / (2(1 - u^2)). That's also rational. So we have a rational parameterization in terms of u = tan(θ/2), v = tan(φ/2), with u,v ∈ ℝ, but for θ ∈ (-π/2, π/2), θ/2 ∈ (-π/4, π/4), so u ∈ (-1, 1). Similarly, v ∈ (-1, 1) because φ ∈ (-π/2, π/2). However we also have conditions like θ < φ and |θ| < |φ|. That translates to something about u and v. But that's okay.

Now we can compute P coordinates in terms of u and v, which might simplify a lot. Let's do that.

Let:

u = tan(θ/2) → θ ∈ (-π/2, π/2) → θ/2 ∈ (-π/4, π/4) → u ∈ (-1, 1). Similarly, v ∈ (-1, 1).

Then:

cos θ = (1 - u^2)/(1 + u^2). sin θ = 2u/(1 + u^2). tan θ = 2u/(1 - u^2). (1)

cos φ = (1 - v^2)/(1 + v^2). sin φ = 2v/(1 + v^2). tan φ = 2v/(1 - v^2). (2)

Now compute c_y = m - r1. But we have expression for c_y in terms of θ: c_y = - (1/2)*(cos θ)/(1 + sin θ). That's simple. Let's compute that using u.

cos θ / (1 + sin θ) = ( (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2) ) / ( 1 + 2u/(1+u^2) ) = ( (1 - u^2)/(1+u^2) ) / ( (1+u^2 + 2u)/(1+u^2) ) = (1 - u^2) / (1+u^2+2u) = (1 - u^2) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = ( (1-u)(1+u) ) / ( (1+u)^2 ) = (1-u)/(1+u).

So c_y = -1/2 * (1-u)/(1+u). Good.

Now compute d_y = n + r2. We have expression d_y = (1/2)*(1+ sin φ)/cos φ. Compute (1+ sin φ)/cos φ using v:

sin φ = 2v/(1+v^2), cos φ = (1 - v^2)/(1+v^2). Then:

1 + sin φ = (1+v^2 + 2v)/(1+v^2) = (1+v)^2/(1+v^2). So (1+ sin φ)/cos φ = ((1+v)^2/(1+v^2)) / ((1 - v^2)/(1+v^2)) = (1+v)^2 / (1 - v^2) = (1+v)^2 / ((1-v)(1+v)) = (1+v)/(1-v). So:

d_y = (1/2) * (1+v)/(1-v). That's nice.

Thus we have:

c_y = - (1/2) * (1 - u) / (1 + u). (1)
d_y = (1/2) * (1 + v) / (1 - v). (2)

Now also m = (1/2) tan θ = (1/2) * (2u/(1 - u^2)) = u/(1 - u^2). Similarly, n = v/(1 - v^2). These are rational.

Now we can compute P = (x_P, y_P) as before:

x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y.
y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2.

Let's compute c_y d_y:

c_y d_y = - (1/2)*(1-u)/(1+u) * (1/2)*(1+v)/(1-v) = - (1/4) * ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ).

Thus:

x_P = 1/4 - c_y d_y = 1/4 + (1/4) * ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) = (1/4) * [ 1 + ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ].

Now compute y_P:

y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2 = ( - (1/2)*(1-u)/(1+u) + (1/2)*(1+v)/(1-v) ) / 2 = (1/2)*( - (1-u)/(1+u) + (1+v)/(1-v) ) / 2 = (1/4)*( - (1-u)/(1+u) + (1+v)/(1-v) ).

Simplify:

y_P = (1/4) * ( (1+v)/(1-v) - (1-u)/(1+u) ). That's neat.

Now we have v = P (vector). So v = (x_P, y_P). That's still messy but perhaps we can find α and β.

Let's compute M·P and N·P. M = (1/2, m) = (1/2, u/(1-u^2)). N = (1/2, n) = (1/2, v/(1-v^2)). That's rational. Then:

M·P = (1/2) x_P + m y_P = (1/2) x_P + (u/(1-u^2)) y_P.
N·P = (1/2) x_P + (v/(1-v^2)) y_P.

Now α = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2. That's a scalar. Since P is a vector, |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2.

Then E = α P, F = β P.

Now B = (1,0). So we have triangle BEF. The line L through H parallel to AP is L: H + t P. We need to prove L is tangent to circumcircle of BEF. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since MN is vertical, H lies on horizontal line y = y_P. So H = (h, y_P). We need to compute h. The expression for h is:

h = 1/2 - ( (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ) / (1/2 - x_P). That's from earlier. But now we have parameterization. Maybe we can compute that expression and simplify. Let's try.

We have:

m = u/(1-u^2). n = v/(1-v^2). y_P = (1/4)*( (1+v)/(1-v) - (1-u)/(1+u) ). Also x_P = (1/4)*( 1 + ((1-u)(1+v))/((1+u)(1-v)) ). That's messy but perhaps simplifies to something like (1/4)*(something). Let's compute x_P in terms of u,v:

x_P = (1/4) * ( 1 + ( (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ). Combine the fraction: Write 1 as ( (1+u)(1-v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ). Then:

x_P = (1/4) * ( ( (1+u)(1-v) + (1-u)(1+v) ) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ).

Compute numerator:

(1+u)(1-v) + (1-u)(1+v) = (1 - v + u - uv) + (1 + v - u - uv) = (1 - v + u - uv) + (1 + v - u - uv) = 2 - 2uv. Because -v + v cancels, u - u cancels, -uv - uv = -2uv. So numerator = 2(1 - uv). Thus:

x_P = (1/4) * ( 2(1 - uv) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ) = (1/2) * ( (1 - uv) / ( (1+u)(1-v) ) ).

So:

x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.   (4)

That's a nice simplification! Good.

Now compute y_P similarly:

y_P = \frac{1}{4} \left( \frac{1+v}{1-v} - \frac{1-u}{1+u} \right).

Combine into a single fraction:

Let’s compute A = \frac{1+v}{1-v} - \frac{1-u}{1+u} = \frac{ (1+v)(1+u) - (1-u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)(1+u) }.

Compute numerator:

(1+v)(1+u) = (1+u+v+uv). (1-u)(1-v) = (1 - u - v + uv). Subtract: (1+u+v+uv) - (1 - u - v + uv) = 1+u+v+uv -1 + u + v - uv = 2u + 2v. So numerator = 2(u+v). Thus:

A = \frac{2(u+v)}{(1-v)(1+u)}.

Therefore:

y_P = \frac{1}{4} * A = \frac{1}{4} * \frac{2(u+v)}{(1-v)(1+u)} = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

So:

y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.   (5)

Great! So P = (x_P, y_P) with:

x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.
y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Now note that (1+u)(1-v) > 0 because u,v ∈ (-1,1) but (1+u) > 0, (1-v) > 0 (since v<1). Indeed, u ∈ (-1,1) → 1+u > 0; v ∈ (-1,1) → 1-v > 0. So denominator positive. So P coordinates are rational functions.

Now also m = u/(1-u^2). We can compute r1 - m expression we had. But maybe we also need (y_P - m) and (n - y_P). Let's compute those.

We have:

m = \frac{u}{1-u^2}. n = \frac{v}{1-v^2}.

We can also compute y_P - m and n - y_P. But perhaps the orthocenter H's x-coordinate simplifies nicely.

Let's compute H. H = (h, y_P). We need h.

First compute 1/2 - x_P. x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. Then:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1 - uv}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} = \frac{1}{2} \left( 1 - \frac{1 - uv}{(1+u)(1-v)} \right). So:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) - (1 - uv) }{ (1+u)(1-v) } = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) - 1 + uv }{ (1+u)(1-v) }.

Compute (1+u)(1-v) = 1 - v + u - uv. Subtract 1 gives -v + u - uv. Then add uv gives -v + u - uv + uv = u - v. So numerator simplifies to u - v. Indeed:

(1+u)(1-v) - (1 - uv) = (1 - v + u - uv) - 1 + uv = u - v. So:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{ u - v }{ (1+u)(1-v) } = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Great. So:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.   (6)

Now compute (y_P - m)*(n - y_P). We have expressions for y_P, m, n. But we earlier derived a simplified expression: (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = 1/4 (Δ^2 - (r2 - r1)^2). That might be messy. Let's compute directly in terms of u,v. However we also have the expression for H: h = 1/2 - ( (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ) / (1/2 - x_P). We can compute (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) using the rational forms. That might simplify nicely.

First, compute y_P - m:

y_P - m = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u^2}.

Similarly, n - y_P = \frac{v}{1-v^2} - \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

y_P - m = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} - \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{1}{1+u} \left( \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u} \right). So that's consistent.

Now compute the bracket B1 = \frac{u+v}{2 (1-v)} - \frac{u}{1-u}. Put over common denominator 2 (1-v)(1-u):

B1 = \frac{ (u+v)(1-u) - 2u (1-v) }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u) }.

We already computed numerator N1 = v - u - u^2 + uv. So:

B1 = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u) }.

Thus:

y_P - m = \frac{1}{1+u} * \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1-v)(1-u) } = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1+u)(1-v)(1-u) }.

Similarly, compute n - y_P:

n = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)}.
y_P = \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

So:

n - y_P = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)} - \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} = \frac{1}{1-v} \left( \frac{v}{1+v} - \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)} \right). Compute bracket B2:

B2 = \frac{v}{1+v} - \frac{u+v}{2 (1+u)} = \frac{ 2v (1+u) - (u+v)(1+v) }{ 2 (1+v)(1+u) }.

We computed numerator N2 = v + uv - u - v^2. So:

B2 = \frac{ v + uv - u - v^2 }{ 2 (1+v)(1+u) }.

Thus:

n - y_P = \frac{1}{1-v} * \frac{ v + uv - u - v^2 }{ 2 (1+v)(1+u) } = \frac{ v + uv - u - v^2 }{ 2 (1-v)(1+v)(1+u) }.

Now product (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ v - u - u^2 + uv }{ 2 (1+u)(1-v)(1-u) } * \frac{ v + uv - u - v^2 }{ 2 (1-v)(1+v)(1+u) } = \frac{ (v - u - u^2 + uv)(v + uv - u - v^2) }{ 4 (1+u)^2 (1-v)^2 (1-u)(1+v) }.

Simplify numerator. Let's denote:

N1 = v - u - u^2 + uv.
N2 = v + uv - u - v^2.

We can factor (v - u) perhaps. Compute N1 - N2? Not needed.

Compute N1 * N2. Let's do it step by step.

N1 = (v - u) + uv - u^2.
N2 = (v - u) + uv - v^2.

Let’s set Δ = v - u. Then N1 = Δ + uv - u^2, N2 = Δ + uv - v^2.

Now N1 * N2 = (Δ + uv)^2 - (Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) + u^2 v^2? Actually (Δ + uv - u^2)(Δ + uv - v^2) = (Δ + uv)^2 - (Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) + u^2 v^2. Because (a - p)(a - q) = a^2 - a(p+q) + pq. Here a = Δ + uv. p = u^2, q = v^2. So:

N1 N2 = (Δ + uv)^2 - (Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) + u^2 v^2.

Now compute Δ = v - u. Then Δ + uv = v - u + uv.

We can expand:

(Δ + uv)^2 = (v - u)^2 + 2 (v - u) uv + (uv)^2 = (v^2 - 2uv + u^2) + 2uv(v - u) + u^2 v^2.
Simplify 2uv(v - u) = 2uv^2 - 2u^2 v. So:

(Δ + uv)^2 = u^2 + v^2 - 2uv + 2uv^2 - 2u^2 v + u^2 v^2.

Now (Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) = (v - u + uv)(u^2 + v^2). Expand: (v - u)(u^2 + v^2) + uv (u^2 + v^2). (v - u)(u^2 + v^2) = v(u^2+v^2) - u(u^2+v^2) = v u^2 + v^3 - u^3 - u v^2. So:

(Δ + uv)(u^2 + v^2) = (v u^2 + v^3 - u^3 - u v^2) + uv(u^2+v^2) = (v u^2 + v^3 - u^3 - u v^2) + u^3 v + u v^3.

Then u^2 v^2 appears.

Now N1 N2 = (Δ + uv)^2 - (Δ + uv)(u^2+v^2) + u^2 v^2.

Plug in and simplify. That seems messy. However perhaps we can notice that (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) simplifies nicely when we compute the ratio needed for h. Since h = 1/2 - ( (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ) / (1/2 - x_P). And we have (1/2 - x_P) = \frac{u - v}{2 D}. So:

2y = c_y + d_y → y = (c_y + d_y)/2. That's consistent with our earlier result. So indeed, y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2.

Then plug back into (1) to get x_P:

Then c_y = m - r1 = u/(1-u^2) - (1+u^2)/(2(1-u^2)) = \frac{2u - (1+u^2)}{2(1-u^2)} = \frac{2u - 1 - u^2}{2(1-u^2)} = \frac{ - (u^2 - 2u + 1) }{2(1-u^2)} = \frac{ - (u-1)^2 }{2(1-u^2)} = \frac{ - (u-1)^2 }{2(1-u^2)}.

But (u-1)^2 = (1-u)^2. And 1-u^2 = (1-u)(1+u). So c_y = \frac{ - (1-u)^2 }{2 (1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{ - (1-u) }{2 (1+u)}. That's a simple expression! Indeed, c_y = - \frac{1-u}{2(1+u)}. That's the same as before but with a factor 1/2? Earlier we had c_y = - (1/2)*(1-u)/(1+u). Let's compare: earlier we had c_y = - (1/2) * (1-u)/(1+u). That's exactly the same. So c_y = - \frac{1-u}{2(1+u)}.

Similarly, d_y = n + r2. n = v/(1-v^2). r2 = (1+v^2)/(2(1-v^2)). Then d_y = \frac{v}{1-v^2} + \frac{1+v^2}{2(1-v^2)} = \frac{2v + (1+v^2)}{2(1-v^2)} = \frac{1 + v^2 + 2v}{2(1-v^2)} = \frac{ (1+v)^2 }{2(1-v^2)} = \frac{ (1+v)^2 }{2 (1-v)(1+v)} = \frac{ 1+v }{2 (1-v) }. So d_y = \frac{1+v}{2(1-v)}. That's the same as before.

Thus we have:

c_y = - \frac{1-u}{2(1+u)}.
d_y = \frac{1+v}{2(1-v)}.

Now compute P's coordinates:

Simplify: Multiply numerator and denominator. That's what we had.

That's what we had.

For u=0.2, v=0.4, we computed (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) ≈ 0.01238. Compute (u - v)^2 / (4 D^2) = ( -0.2)^2 / (4 * 0.72^2) = 0.04 / (4 * 0.5184) = 0.04 / 2.0736 ≈ 0.0193. That's larger. So not that. Compute (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 over 4. Let's compute n - m numerically: n=0.47619, m=0.20833, difference = 0.26786. r2 = 1/(2 cos φ) with φ? Actually we can compute r2 from n: r2 = sqrt(1/4 + n^2) ≈ sqrt(0.25 + 0.2268) ≈ sqrt(0.4768) ≈ 0.6904. r1 = sqrt(0.25 + 0.0434) ≈ sqrt(0.2934) ≈ 0.5419. Then (n - m)^2 ≈ 0.0718, (r2 - r1)^2 ≈ (0.1485)^2 ≈ 0.02205, difference ≈ 0.04975, divided by 4 ≈ 0.01244. That's close to 0.01238. So indeed (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = ((n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2)/4 ≈ 0.01244.

Now 1/2 - x_P = (u - v)/(2 D) ≈ -0.2/(2*0.72) = -0.2/1.44 ≈ -0.13889. Then h = 1/2 - product/(1/2 - x_P) ≈ 0.5 - 0.01244/(-0.13889) = 0.5 + 0.0897 = 0.5897. So h is around 0.5897. That seems plausible.

Now we need to prove that the line L through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). Since we have expressions for B, E, F, and H, perhaps we can compute the equation of the circle (BEF) and then compute the distance from its center to L. That seems heavy, but maybe we can find a relation: The circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter? Let's compute the coordinates of E and F in terms of u,v. We have P = (x_P, y_P). Then α = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2. M·P = (1/2) x_P + m y_P. Similarly N·P. So we need to compute M·P and N·P in terms of u,v. Then E = α P, F = β P. That's fine.

But perhaps there is a more clever synthetic solution. Let's try to find a relationship involving the orthocenter H and the line through H parallel to AP. Since AP is a line through A and P, and P is the circumcenter of ACD. Maybe we can consider the circle with diameter AP? Not.

Another idea: Use coordinates but simplify by setting A = (0,0) and B = (1,0). Then the line MN is x = 1/2. Then we have M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n). Then we derived simple rational expressions for P in terms of u,v. That's good. Now we also have expressions for m and n in terms of u,v. So we can compute M·P, N·P. That might simplify nicely. Then we can compute α and β. Then we can compute the circle (BEF). However we also need H. Let's attempt to compute H using the formula h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). But we have expression for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) that involves n - m and r2 - r1. Perhaps we can also express (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) in terms of (u - v) and (something) by computing n - m and r2 - r1 explicitly with rational forms. Let's do that.

We have:

m = \frac{u}{1-u^2}
n = \frac{v}{1-v^2}

Also, r1 = \frac{1+u^2}{2(1-u^2)}. r2 = \frac{1+v^2}{2(1-v^2)}.

Now compute n - m:

n - m = \frac{v}{1-v^2} - \frac{u}{1-u^2}.

To combine, find common denominator: (1-v^2)(1-u^2). Then:

n - m = \frac{ v(1-u^2) - u(1-v^2) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Compute numerator:

v(1-u^2) - u(1-v^2) = v - v u^2 - u + u v^2 = (v - u) + uv^2 - u^2 v = (v - u) + uv(v - u) = (v - u)(1 + uv).

Thus:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.   (4)

Now compute r2 - r1:

r2 - r1 = \frac{1+v^2}{2(1-v^2)} - \frac{1+u^2}{2(1-u^2)} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1+v^2}{1-v^2} - \frac{1+u^2}{1-u^2} \right).

Combine the fractions inside: \frac{1+v^2}{1-v^2} - \frac{1+u^2}{1-u^2}. Let's compute common denominator (1-v^2)(1-u^2). Then:

\frac{ (1+v^2)(1-u^2) - (1+u^2)(1-v^2) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Compute numerator:

(1+v^2)(1-u^2) - (1+u^2)(1-v^2) = (1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2) - (1 - v^2 + u^2 - u^2 v^2) = (1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2) - 1 + v^2 - u^2 + u^2 v^2 = combine: 1 - 1 cancels, -u^2 - u^2 = -2u^2? Wait careful: (1+v^2)(1-u^2) = 1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2. (1+u^2)(1-v^2) = 1 - v^2 + u^2 - u^2 v^2. Subtract: (1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2) - (1 - v^2 + u^2 - u^2 v^2) = 1 - u^2 + v^2 - u^2 v^2 - 1 + v^2 - u^2 + u^2 v^2 = (-u^2 - u^2) + (v^2 + v^2) + (-u^2 v^2 + u^2 v^2) = -2u^2 + 2v^2.

Thus:

\frac{1+v^2}{1-v^2} - \frac{1+u^2}{1-u^2} = \frac{2(v^2 - u^2)}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) } = \frac{2(v-u)(v+u)}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Therefore:

r2 - r1 = \frac{1}{2} * \frac{2(v-u)(v+u)}{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) } = \frac{ (v-u)(v+u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.   (5)

Now note that (v-u) = -(u-v). So we have expressions for n - m and r2 - r1. Now we can compute (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2.

We have:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.
r2 - r1 = \frac{ (v - u)(v + u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Thus both have the same factor (v - u) / ((1-v^2)(1-u^2)). Let’s denote:

K = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Then:

n - m = K * (1 + uv).
r2 - r1 = K * (v + u).

Now compute:

(n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = K^2 [ (1 + uv)^2 - (v + u)^2 ].

Simplify (1 + uv)^2 - (v + u)^2:

(1 + uv)^2 = 1 + 2uv + u^2 v^2.
(v + u)^2 = u^2 + 2uv + v^2.

So difference = 1 + 2uv + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - 2uv - v^2 = 1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2.

Thus:

(n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = K^2 * (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2).

Now we have:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4} = \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now compute K^2:

K^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Now we also have (1/2 - x_P) = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. So (u - v) = -(v - u). But we'll keep.

Now h = 1/2 - \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P }.

Plug in:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 D}, where D = (1+u)(1-v). So 1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Now (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Thus:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} }{ \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} } = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v }.

Simplify: \frac{2}{4} = \frac{1}{2}. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ K^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ u - v }.

Now K^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } because square. So we can write (v - u)^2 = (u - v)^2.

Thus:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ u - v }.

Note that (u - v)^2 / (u - v) = (u - v). So we have:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{? Actually we have factor (1+u)(1-v) in numerator. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * (1+u)(1-v).

But we can also simplify (1-v^2) = (1-v)(1+v), and (1-u^2) = (1-u)(1+u). So:

(1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 = (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2.

Thus:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1+u)(1-v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 }.

Cancel one (1+u) and one (1-v) maybe? Let's simplify:

\frac{ (u - v) (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) } * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{(1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 }? Actually we have denominator (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2. Numerator (u - v) (1+u)(1-v). So we can cancel one (1-v) from denominator, leaving (1-v) in denominator. Also cancel one (1+u) from denominator, leaving (1+u) in denominator. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

But careful: after canceling, denominator becomes (1-v) * (1+v)^2 * (1-u)^2 * (1+u). Yes.

So h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Now this seems still messy. However note that we also have expressions for m and n, and we might compute the coordinates of E and F in terms of u,v. Then the circle (BEF) maybe simplifies and the tangency condition becomes something like (h - 1/2) is something that makes L tangent. But this is heavy.

Perhaps we can find a different parameterization that makes P simpler. The earlier param with u and v gave P in a simple rational form. That's good. Now perhaps we can also find that H has a simple expression in terms of u and v. Let's try to compute h directly using the formula h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). We already have an expression for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) in terms of K and (1+u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2). And we have (1/2 - x_P) expressed. Maybe the ratio simplifies to something like \frac{ (1+u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) (u - v) }{2 (1+u)(1-v) (something)}. Let's try to compute h more directly with the rational forms, but maybe we can find a pattern by computing M·P and N·P, then find α and β, then the circle (BEF) maybe has a simple equation, like (x - something)^2 + (y - something)^2 = something, and then L's distance is radius. But perhaps we can prove that H is the midpoint of the segment connecting the projections of B onto AP? That seems unlikely.

Another thought: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, the line AP is the line through A and the circumcenter. The line through H parallel to AP is L. Maybe we can show that L is the Simson line of some point with respect to triangle BEF. Actually the Simson line of a point on the circumcircle of a triangle is a line that passes through the feet of perpendiculars. If we take the point P (maybe P is on the circumcircle of BEF?) That could be interesting. Let's check if P lies on (BEF). P is the circumcenter of ACD. Not obviously related. But maybe we can prove that P, B, E, F are concyclic? Let's examine. E and F are on AP.
