Given the difficulty, perhaps we should attempt a synthetic approach. Let's try to understand the configuration in terms of known lemmas.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersect at A and B. Let’s denote the midpoint of AB as O_AB, which lies on MN. Actually the line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. So the midpoint M_AB of AB is on MN. But M and N are centers. So M_AB is the foot of perpendicular from M and N to AB? Actually M_AB is the point where MN intersects AB? No, MN is perpendicular to AB and passes through the midpoint of AB. So M_AB is the midpoint of AB. So we have M_AB ∈ MN.

Now consider triangle ACD. Its circumcenter P. We have AP is a line. The line through H parallel to AP is L. H is orthocenter of PMN. So PH is a line (horizontal). Since PH is perpendicular to MN, and AP is not necessarily.

Maybe we can prove that L is the reflection of AP across MN? That would be parallel to AP? Actually reflection of AP across MN would be a line symmetric; it would be parallel to AP if AP is perpendicular to MN? Not. But we need L ∥ AP. So L is a translate.

Let's compute H's coordinates with the rational expressions we have. Perhaps we can find that h is something like \frac{1}{2} - \frac{(1-u)(1+v)}{4 (1+u)(1-v)}? Not sure.

Let's compute using the simplified expressions for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) and 1/2 - x_P, but also express (n - m) and (r2 - r1) in terms of u and v. Then compute the ratio and simplify.

We have:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. (A)

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4}. We expressed:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.
r2 - r1 = \frac{ (v - u)(v + u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

So define:

Δ = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }. Then n - m = Δ (1+uv), r2 - r1 = Δ (v+u). Then:

(n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = Δ^2 [ (1+uv)^2 - (v+u)^2 ] = Δ^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2). So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ Δ^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now compute Δ^2:

Δ^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Thus:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now (1-v^2) = (1-v)(1+v), (1-u^2) = (1-u)(1+u). So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now (1/2 - x_P) = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. So:

\frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} }{ \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v }.

Simplify: \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ u - v } = u - v. So we have:

\frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ (u - v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * 2 (1+u)(1-v).

= \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * 2 (1+u)(1-v)?? Actually we have factor 2 (1+u)(1-v) in numerator. So:

= \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 }.

Simplify further: \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ 1 }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Thus:

\frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ 1 }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Now h = 1/2 - that expression. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (u - v) (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

That's what we had. So h is something like 1/2 minus a fraction.

Now the line L through H parallel to AP (direction P) is L: H + t P. Since P is not a unit vector. So L's direction is (x_P, y_P). So L has slope y_P / x_P if x_P != 0. But maybe we can compute the distance from the center of (BEF) to L and then show it equals radius. That seems heavy. But perhaps we can find that (BEF) is actually the circle with diameter something like the segment connecting B and the midpoint of EF? Not.

Let's try to compute the circle (BEF) using the fact that E and F are multiples of P. Then the circle passing through B and those points can be expressed as the set of points X such that (X - B) · v? Actually we can find the equation of the circle in terms of P and B. Since E = α P and F = β P, we can param. The circle (BEF) is the circumcircle of triangle B, α P, β P. Its center O_BEF is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting α P and β P is the line perpendicular to P passing through ((α+β)/2) P. So O_BEF lies on that line. Also, O_BEF is equidistant to B and α P. So O_BEF is the center.

We can find O_BEF coordinates by solving the system. Let’s denote v = P = (a,b). Then α P = (α a, α b). β P = (β a, β b). B = (1,0). We need to find O = (x,y) such that:

|O - B|^2 = |O - α P|^2 = |O - β P|^2.

From |O - B|^2 = |O - α P|^2:

(x - 1)^2 + y^2 = (x - α a)^2 + (y - α b)^2.

Expand:

x^2 - 2x + 1 + y^2 = x^2 - 2α a x + α^2 a^2 + y^2 - 2α b y + α^2 b^2.

Cancel x^2 + y^2:

-2x + 1 = -2α a x + α^2 (a^2 + b^2) - 2α b y.

Bring terms:

-2x + 1 + 2α a x + 2α b y = α^2 |v|^2.

So:

2α (a x + b y) - 2x + 1 = α^2 |v|^2.   (1)

Similarly, from |O - B|^2 = |O - β P|^2:

-2x + 1 = -2β a x + β^2 |v|^2 - 2β b y. => 2β (a x + b y) - 2x + 1 = β^2 |v|^2.   (2)

Now also from |O - α P|^2 = |O - β P|^2:

(x - α a)^2 + (y - α b)^2 = (x - β a)^2 + (y - β b)^2. This simplifies to:

-2α a x - 2α b y + α^2|v|^2 = -2β a x - 2β b y + β^2|v|^2. => 2(β - α) (a x + b y) = (β^2 - α^2)|v|^2. That's an equation.

But perhaps we can find O explicitly. Since we know that the perpendicular bisector of α P and β P is the line: (x,y) = ((α+β)/2) v + s * v_perp. So O lies on that line. Also, O must satisfy the equation with B. So we can param O = ((α+β)/2) v + t * v_perp, where v_perp is a vector perpendicular to v. Then plug into |O - B|^2 = |O - α P|^2. That might yield t. That seems plausible. However the expressions might become messy.

Given the complexity, maybe there is a better synthetic approach. Let's search for known properties: In a triangle, the line through the orthocenter parallel to a side is tangent to the nine-point circle? Actually the nine-point circle has radius half the circumradius. The line through the orthocenter parallel to a side is not generally tangent. Another idea: Use coordinates but choose a coordinate system where AP is vertical, i.e., a line through A and P is vertical. Then L is horizontal. Coordinates:

Now P = (x_P, y_P) as above. Then AP is line through (0,0) and P. So its direction vector is (x_P, y_P). Then line L through H parallel to AP has direction (x_P, y_P). So L is a line with slope y_P / x_P if x_P != 0. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN.

We have M = (1/2, m), N = (1/2, n), P = (x_P, y_P). Since MN is vertical, the altitude from P is horizontal, so H has y-coordinate y_P. So H = (h, y_P). Then L is the line through H with direction (x_P, y_P). So L param: H + t (x_P, y_P). So L's slope is y_P / x_P.

Now we need to prove that L is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. B = (1,0). E and F are on AP (line through origin and P). So E = α P, F = β P, where α = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2, β = 2 (N·P) / |P|^2. That's derived.

Now the circle (BEF) is the circumcircle of triangle with vertices (1,0), α P, β P. Its equation can be found. Let v = P. Then E = α v, F = β v. So the points B, α v, β v. Since α and β are scalars. The circle passing through these points is determined. The perpendicular bisector is the line through that point perpendicular to v. So O lies on that line. Also, O is equidistant to B. So O = ((α+β)/2) v + t * v_perp, where v_perp is a vector perpendicular to v. Then we can compute t such that |O - B|^2 = |O - α v|^2. That will give an equation. That seems promising. Then the line L is H + s v. We need to prove that L is tangent to the circle. That is, the distance from O to L equals R. The distance from O to L is |(O - H) × v| / |v| (if we consider line with direction v). Since L is parallel to v, its distance from O is the perpendicular distance. So we need to compute |(O - H) × v| / |v| = R. That's a scalar equation. But maybe we can find a relationship: The line L passes through H and is parallel to AP. The circle (BEF) has chord EF on AP. So the line through H parallel to AP is a line that is parallel to the chord EF. If that line is tangent, then it must be at a distance equal to the radius. I think the analytic approach with u,v is doable, but we need to compute O and R and then check the tangency condition. However the expressions might simplify nicely because many terms are rational and the cross product might become something like (something) * (u - v) etc. Let's try to compute step by step.

First, define u,v ∈ (-1,1). Let’s denote:

D = (1+u)(1-v). Then:

x_P = \frac{1 - uv}{2D}
y_P = \frac{u+v}{2D}

Also, m = \frac{u}{1-u^2} = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)}. n = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)}.

We'll need M·P and N·P. Compute M·P:

M·P = \frac12 x_P + m y_P.

Compute \frac12 x_P = \frac12 * \frac{1-uv}{2D} = \frac{1-uv}{4D}.

m y_P = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)} * \frac{u+v}{2D} = \frac{u(u+v)}{2D (1-u)(1+u)}.

Simplify denominator: (1-u)(1+u) = 1-u^2. So m y_P = \frac{u(u+v)}{2D (1-u^2)}.

Similarly, N·P = \frac12 x_P + n y_P = \frac{1-uv}{4D} + \frac{v(v+u)}{2D (1-v^2)}? Actually n = v/(1-v^2), so n y_P = \frac{v}{1-v^2} * \frac{u+v}{2D} = \frac{v(u+v)}{2D (1-v^2)}.

Now we also have |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2 = \frac{ (1-uv)^2 + (u+v)^2 }{4 D^2} = \frac{ 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2 }{4 D^2} as derived. Let's denote S = 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2.

Now α = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2 = \frac{2 (M·P)}{|P|^2}. So E = α P. Similarly for F.

Now we need to compute H. H = (h, y_P) with h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). But we also have an expression for (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) in terms of n,m,r1,r2. That gave (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4}. We have formulas for n - m and r2 - r1. That seems manageable. However we might also compute h directly from the linear system of altitudes: (H - M) · (N - P) = 0, and H's y = y_P. That gave h = 1/2 - ((y_P - m)*(n - y_P)) / (1/2 - x_P). We'll compute that.

Let’s compute (y_P - m) and (n - y_P) in terms of u,v, perhaps simpler than the (n - m) expression. We already have:

y_P - m = \frac{n - m + r2 - r1}{2}? Actually earlier we derived:

y_P = (c_y + d_y)/2 = (m - r1 + n + r2)/2 = \frac{m + n + r2 - r1}{2}. So:

y_P - m = \frac{m + n + r2 - r1}{2} - m = \frac{-m + n + r2 - r1}{2} = \frac{n - m + r2 - r1}{2}. (1)

n - y_P = n - \frac{m + n + r2 - r1}{2} = \frac{2n - m - n - r2 + r1}{2} = \frac{n - m - r2 + r1}{2}. (2)

Thus:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ (n - m + r2 - r1) (n - m - r2 + r1) }{4} = \frac{ (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 }{4}. That's correct.

Now we have expressions for n - m and r2 - r1. We'll compute those.

We have:

n - m = \frac{ (v - u)(1 + uv) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

r2 - r1 = \frac{ (v - u)(v + u) }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

So both have factor (v - u) / ((1-v^2)(1-u^2)). Let’s denote:

F = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }.

Then n - m = F (1+uv), r2 - r1 = F (v+u). Then (n - m)^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = F^2 [ (1+uv)^2 - (u+v)^2 ] = F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2). So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4}.

Now compute F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Also, 1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 D} = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}. Since u - v = -(v - u), but squared is same. So we have:

1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)}.

Now compute the ratio:

R = \frac{ (y_P - m)*(n - y_P) }{ 1/2 - x_P } = \frac{ \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} }{ \frac{u - v}{2 (1+u)(1-v)} } = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{4} * \frac{2 (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v } = \frac{ F^2 (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ (1+u)(1-v)}{ u - v }.

Now F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

So \frac{ F^2 }{ u - v } = \frac{ (u - v)^2 }{ (u - v) } * \frac{1}{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } = \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }.

Thus:

R = \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 } * (1+u)(1-v).

Now note that (1-v^2) = (1-v)(1+v). So (1-v^2)^2 = (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2. Then:

R = \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } * (1+u)(1-v).

Simplify: \frac{ (1+u)(1-v) }{ (1-v)^2 (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u)^2 } = \frac{ 1 }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Thus:

R = \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) }{2} * \frac{ u - v }{ (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

Now h = 1/2 - R. So:

h = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{ (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) (u - v) }{ 2 (1-v) (1+v)^2 (1-u)^2 (1+u) }.

This is messy. But maybe we can simplify further by noting that (1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2) = (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) - 2u^2 v^2? Actually (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = 1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2. That's 1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2. So our expression is 1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2 = (1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2). That's exactly (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2). Wait compute (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = 1 - u^2 - v^2 + u^2 v^2. Yes! That's exactly our numerator. So:

1 + u^2 v^2 - u^2 - v^2 = (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2). Good catch! That simplifies a lot. So:

(y_P - m)*(n - y_P) = \frac{ F^2 (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) }{4}.

Now F = \frac{ v - u }{ (1-v^2)(1-u^2) }. So F^2 = \frac{ (v - u)^2 }{ (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 }. Then:

That's much simpler! Let's double-check: (v - u)^2 / ( (1-v^2)^2 (1-u^2)^2 ) * (1 - u^2)(1 - v^2) = (v - u)^2 / ( (1-v^2)(1-u^2) ) * (1 / ( (1-v^2)(1-u^2) ))? Wait careful:

That's not correct because we have (1-v^2)^2 in denominator, multiplying by (1 - v^2) yields (1-v^2) in denominator, but we have (1-v^2) in numerator, so one factor cancels, leaving (1-v^2) in denominator. Actually in complex, M·P is not directly dot product; but the formula for second intersection we derived using squared distances is valid in complex as well, but we need to be careful. In complex numbers, for a line through origin with direction v, points are t v. The condition |t v - M|^2 = |M|^2 gives t^2 |v|^2 - 2 t Re(M \overline{v}) = 0. So t = 2 Re(M \overline{v}) / |v|^2. So we can use complex numbers.

Now compute M·P:

\frac{u(u+v)}{2 D (1-u)(1+u)} = \frac{u(u+v)}{2 D (1-u^2)}.

Put over common denominator 4 D (1-u^2). Then:

Now compute numerator:

N_M = (1-uv)(1-u^2) + 2u(u+v). Expand (1-uv)(1-u^2) = (1-uv) - (1-uv)u^2 = (1-uv) - u^2 + u^3 v. So:

N_M = 1 - uv - u^2 + u^3 v + 2u(u+v) = 1 - uv - u^2 + u^3 v + 2u^2 + 2uv = 1 + (-uv + 2uv) + (-u^2 + 2u^2) + u^3 v = 1 + uv + u^2 + u^3 v.

So N_M = 1 + u^2 + uv + u^3 v.

Thus:

M·P = \frac{ 1 + u^2 + uv + u^3 v }{4 D (1-u^2)}.

Similarly, N·P:

N = (1/2, n). n = \frac{v}{1-v^2} = \frac{v}{(1-v)(1+v)}.

N·P = \frac{ (1-uv)(1-v^2) + 2v(u+v) }{4 D (1-v^2)}.

So N_N = 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3.

Thus:

N·P = \frac{ 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3 }{4 D (1-v^2)}.

Now |P|^2 = \frac{ 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2 }{4 D^2}. So:

Because 4 D^2 / (4 D) = D. So:

α = 2 D * \frac{ 1 + u^2 + uv + u^3 v }{ (1-u^2) T }.

Similarly, β = 2 (N·P) / |P|^2 = 2 * \frac{ 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3 }{4 D (1-v^2)} * \frac{4 D^2}{ T } = 2 D * \frac{ 1 + uv + v^2 + u v^3 }{ (1-v^2) T }.

Now note that T = 1 + u^2 v^2 + u^2 + v^2. That's symmetric.

Given the complexity, I'm leaning towards the idea that the analytic solution with A=(0,0), B=(1,0) is doable, and we can compute the circle (BEF) and the line L and prove tangency using algebraic identities. The expressions for α and β are messy, but perhaps we can simplify by noticing that α and β are such that (α+β)/2 and (β-α)/2 have nice forms. Let's compute α and β more directly using the formulas for second intersection we derived earlier: For a point X on line through A with direction v, the second intersection with circle centered at M (with A on circle) is given by t = 2 Re(M \overline{v}) / |v|^2. So α = 2 Re(M \overline{P}) / |P|^2. Similarly β = 2 Re(N \overline{P}) / |P|^2. So we need Re(M \overline{P}) and Re(N \overline{P}). Since we have expressions for M and P in terms of u,v, we can compute Re(M \overline{P}) = dot product. That's what we did. So α and β are rational.

Now maybe we can compute the circle (BEF) using the formula for the circle passing through two points on a line and a third point. There is a known formula: The equation of the circle through points (1,0) and (e,0) and (f,0) would be something like (x - something)^2 + (y - something)^2 = something. But here E and F are not on the x-axis; they are on AP, which is not horizontal. So that's not simpler.

I think we need to try a different tactic: Use coordinates but with A at origin and B at (1,0). Then we can compute the circle (BEF) as the circumcircle of triangle with vertices (1,0), αP, βP. Since αP and βP are collinear with the origin, we can find the equation of the circle by using the fact that it's the circle that passes through those three points. The center O can be expressed as something like: O = \frac{ (α+β) }{2} P + \frac{ (α - β) }{2} P⊥? Not sure. Let's derive using vector methods.

Let v = P. Then we have three points: B, αv, βv. The circle passing through them can be described by the equation:

|X - O|^2 = R^2, O = something.

Alternatively, we can find O by solving the system:

(O - B)·(O - B) = O - αv)·(O - αv) = O - βv)·(O - βv).

From O - αv and O - βv, we get:

|O - αv|^2 = |O - βv|^2 → (O·O - 2α O·v + α^2|v|^2) = (O·O - 2β O·v + β^2|v|^2) → -2α O·v + α^2|v|^2 = -2β O·v + β^2|v|^2 → 2(β - α) O·v = (β^2 - α^2)|v|^2 = (β - α)(β + α)|v|^2. If β ≠ α, divide by (β - α): 2 O·v = (α + β)|v|^2. So:

O·v = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2.   (1)

Thus O lies on the line (the set of points whose dot product with v is constant). That's a line perpendicular to v (since v is fixed). Actually the set {X | X·v = c} is a line (hyperplane) perpendicular to v. So O is on that line. Also, from |O - B|^2 = |O - αv|^2 we get:

|O - B|^2 = |O - αv|^2 → (O·O - 2 O·B + |B|^2) = (O·O - 2α O·v + α^2|v|^2). Cancel O·O:

-2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2α O·v + α^2|v|^2. So:

2 O·v - 2 O·B = α^2|v|^2 - |B|^2. Or:

2 O·(v - B) = α^2|v|^2 - |B|^2.   (2)

Similarly, using β would give another equation, but they should be consistent given (1). We can use (1) to express O·v in terms of α,β,|v|^2. Then plug into (2) to get an equation involving O·B. That's a linear equation in O (since O·B is dot product). So we have two linear equations: O·v = constant, and O·(v - B) = constant. That's two equations; they determine O uniquely up to? Actually O is a 2D vector. The equations O·v = c1 and O·(v - B) = c2 are two linear equations, which can be solved for O. So we can find O explicitly. That seems promising!

We have:

- v = P = (a,b).
- B = (1,0).
- |v|^2 = a^2 + b^2.
- α and β are scalars.

Then from (1): O·v = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2. Let’s denote C1 = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2.

From (2): 2 O·(v - B) = α^2 |v|^2 - |B|^2. Since |B|^2 = 1. So:

O·(v - B) = \frac{ α^2 |v|^2 - 1 }{2}.

Now note that v - B is a vector. So we have two linear equations. That's enough to solve for O. Then we can compute R = |O - B|. Then we need to prove that the line L through H parallel to v is tangent. The distance from O to L is |det(O - H, v)| / |v|. That's another expression. But perhaps we can also find that O lies on a line that is the reflection of something, and then L is tangent because O is on the circle with diameter something? Let's compute O explicitly.

Let’s denote:

v = (a,b). Then O·v = a O_x + b O_y = C1. Also, O·(v - B) = O·(v - (1,0)) = a O_x + b O_y - O_x = (a-1) O_x + b O_y = C2.

So we have:

(1) a O_x + b O_y = C1.
(2) (a-1) O_x + b O_y = C2.

Subtract (1)-(2): (a - (a-1)) O_x = (1) O_x = C1 - C2. So O_x = C1 - C2. Then O_y can be obtained from (1): b O_y = C1 - a O_x → O_y = (C1 - a O_x) / b, provided b ≠ 0. If b = 0, then P is horizontal, and AP is horizontal. That would be a special case where AP is horizontal. In that case, the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal, and we need to prove tangency. That case can be handled separately. So we can assume b ≠ 0 for generic configuration. Also, if b = 0, then P is horizontal, so AP is horizontal. Then L is horizontal. We can check if that case is allowed by the problem? It might be, if the configuration leads to P horizontal. But we can treat it as a separate case and prove that the statement holds (maybe by continuity or by direct computation). So we can focus on b ≠ 0.

Now O_x = C1 - C2. That's simple. So the center O is determined by O_x and O_y.

Now we need to show that the line L: H + t v is tangent to this circle. The distance from O to L is |det(O - H, v)| / |v|. Let's compute O - H. H = (h, y_P). So O - H = (O_x - h, O_y - y_P). Then det(O - H, v) = (O_x - h) b - (O_y - y_P) a. Since det((x,y),(a,b)) = x b - y a.

Thus we need to prove:

| (O_x - h) b - (O_y - y_P) a | / |v| = |O - B|.   (T)

Now we have expressions for O_x, O_y, h, y_P, a, b. Also, α and β are expressed in terms of u,v. So this becomes an equation in u,v. Maybe it simplifies.

Let's compute O_x and O_y in terms of α,β, a,b. We have:

C1 = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2.
C2 = \frac{α^2 |v|^2 - 1}{2}.

Then O_x = C1 - C2 = \frac{α + β}{2} |v|^2 - \frac{α^2 |v|^2 - 1}{2} = \frac{ (α + β) |v|^2 - α^2 |v|^2 + 1 }{2} = \frac{ (α + β - α^2) |v|^2 + 1 }{2}.

Similarly, we can compute O_y from a O_x + b O_y = C1. But maybe we can also find O_y by solving the linear system. Since we already have O_x, then O_y = (C1 - a O_x) / b.

Now also, H = (h, y_P). We have h = 1/2 - \frac{ u - v }{2 (1+v) (1-u) }? Actually we had h = 1/2 - R, where R = \frac{u - v}{2 (1+v) (1-u)}. That's not symmetric. But maybe we can find a relationship between O_x, O_y, h, y_P that makes the determinant equation hold.

Now also, from the condition that O is equidistant to B and αv, we could also find O_x in terms of α and β maybe simpler using the formula for the circumcenter of triangle with two points on a line. There is a formula: The center of the circle through points (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3) can be expressed. But maybe we can compute O_x and O_y directly from α,β, a,b using vector algebra. We have three points: B = (1,0), A1 = α v, A2 = β v. Since A1 and A2 are collinear with origin, we can find the circle's equation by noting that the circle's chord A1A2 is along v. The perpendicular bisector of A1A2 is a line L1: (X - ((α+β)/2 v)) · v = 0. So L1 consists of points X such that (X·v) = ((α+β)/2) |v|^2. So O must satisfy O·v = ((α+β)/2) |v|^2. That's our C1. So O·v = C1. Good.

Also, O must be equidistant to B and A1. So |O - B|^2 = |O - α v|^2 → O·O - 2 O·B + 1 = O·O - 2α O·v + α^2 |v|^2 → -2 O·B + 1 = -2α O·v + α^2 |v|^2. So 2 O·v - 2 O·B = α^2 |v|^2 - 1. That's our C2 equation: O·(v - B) = (α^2 |v|^2 - 1)/2.

a x + b y = C1.   (1)
(a-1) x + b y = C2.   (2)

We need to show that |det(O - H, v)| / |v| = |O - B|. That's one equation.

## Solution
We will prove the statement using analytic geometry.  Without loss of generality we place the configuration as follows.

Let \(A=(0,0)\) and \(B=(1,0)\).  The circles \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\) intersect at \(A\) and \(B\); therefore the line \(MN\) (the line joining the centres) is the perpendicular bisector of \(AB\).  Hence \(MN\) is the vertical line \(x=\frac12\).  Write the centres as
\[
M=\Bigl(\frac12,\,m\Bigr),\qquad N=\Bigl(\frac12,\,n\Bigr)
\]
with \(m,n\in\mathbb R\) and \(m\neq n\) (otherwise the circles would coincide).  The radii are
\[
r_1=|M-A|=\sqrt{\frac14+m^2},\qquad r_2=|N-A|=\sqrt{\frac14+n^2}.
\]

The line \(MN\) meets \(\Omega\) at \(C\) and \(\Gamma\) at \(D\); because the order on \(MN\) is \(C,M,N,D\) (from the problem statement), we have
\[
C=\Bigl(\frac12,\;m-r_1\Bigr),\qquad D=\Bigl(\frac12,\;n+r_2\Bigr).
\]

Now \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\).  Using the equations \(|PA|=|PC|=|PD|\) we obtain
\[
P=\Bigl(\frac14-c_y d_y,\;\frac{c_y+d_y}{2}\Bigr),
\]
where \(c_y=m-r_1\) and \(d_y=n+r_2\).  (The derivation is standard: from \(|PA|^2=|PC|^2\) we get \(x=\frac14-2c_y y+c_y^2\); from \(|PA|^2=|PD|^2\) we get \(x=\frac14-2d_y y+d_y^2\); equating these gives \(y=\frac{c_y+d_y}{2}\) and then \(x=\frac14-c_y d_y\).)

To simplify the expressions we introduce parameters
\[
u=\tan\frac{\theta}{2},\qquad v=\tan\frac{\phi}{2},
\]
where \(\theta\) and \(\phi\) are the angles that the vectors \(MA\) and \(NA\) make with the horizontal.  Then
\[
m=\frac{u}{1-u^2},\qquad n=\frac{v}{1-v^2},\qquad r_1=\frac{1+u^2}{2(1-u^2)},\qquad r_2=\frac{1+v^2}{2(1-v^2)}.
\]
(Indeed, from \(\frac12=r_1\cos\theta,\;m=r_1\sin\theta\) we obtain \(m=\frac12\tan\theta\) and then substituting \(u=\tan\frac\theta2\) yields \(m=\frac{u}{1-u^2}\); similarly \(r_1=\frac{1}{2\cos\theta}=\frac{1+u^2}{2(1-u^2)}\).)

With these we compute
\[
c_y=m-r_1=-\frac{1-u}{2(1+u)},\qquad d_y=n+r_2=\frac{1+v}{2(1-v)}.
\]
Now
\[
D=(1+u)(1-v),
\]
and we find
\[
x_P=\frac14-c_y d_y=\frac{1-uv}{2D},\qquad y_P=\frac{c_y+d_y}{2}=\frac{u+v}{2D}.
\]

Thus
\[
P=\Bigl(\frac{1-uv}{2D},\;\frac{u+v}{2D}\Bigr),\qquad D=(1+u)(1-v).
\]

---

### Orthocentre \(H\) of \(\triangle PMN\)

Since \(M\) and \(N\) lie on the vertical line \(x=\frac12\), the altitude from \(P\) to \(MN\) is horizontal; therefore the orthocentre \(H\) has the same \(y\)-coordinate as \(P\), i.e. \(y_H=y_P\).  Let \(H=(h,y_P)\).  The condition that \(MH\perp PN\) gives
\[
(H-M)\cdot(N-P)=0.
\]
Computing:
\[
H-M=(h-\tfrac12,\;0),\qquad N-P=\Bigl(\tfrac12-x_P,\;n-y_P\Bigr).
\]
Thus
\[
(h-\tfrac12)(\tfrac12-x_P)+(0)(n-y_P)=0\quad\Longrightarrow\quad h-\tfrac12 = \frac{y_P-n}{?}
\]
Wait, we should derive correctly.  Actually we have:

\((H-M)\cdot(N-P)=0\) yields
\[
(h-\tfrac12)(\tfrac12-x_P)+ (y_P-m)(n-y_P)=0.
\]
But careful: \(H-M=(h-\frac12, y_P-m)\).  Because \(H\) has y-coordinate \(y_P\), and \(M\) has y-coordinate \(m\).  So we get:
\[
(h-\tfrac12)(\tfrac12-x_P)+(y_P-m)(n-y_P)=0.
\]

This equation determines \(h\).  However it is easier to use the formula for the orthocentre in terms of the coordinates when two vertices are on a vertical line.  We can also compute \(h\) by solving the linear system from the two altitudes.  Let's do it systematically.

We have \(P=(x_P,y_P)\).  Since \(M\) and \(N\) are on the line \(x=\frac12\), the altitude from \(P\) is horizontal; thus \(H\) lies on the line \(y=y_P\).  So \(H=(h,y_P)\).  The altitude from \(M\) is perpendicular to \(PN\).  So we require
\[
(H-M)\cdot(N-P)=0.
\]
Now \(H-M=(h-\frac12,\;y_P-m)\).  And \(N-P=(\frac12-x_P,\;n-y_P)\).  So
\[
(h-\tfrac12)(\tfrac12-x_P)+(y_P-m)(n-y_P)=0. \tag{1}
\]

Similarly, the altitude from \(N\) gives the same condition (since it's consistent).  So we can solve for \(h\):
\[
h-\tfrac12 = -\frac{(y_P-m)(n-y_P)}{\tfrac12-x_P}.
\]
Thus
\[
h = \frac12 - \frac{(y_P-m)(n-y_P)}{\tfrac12-x_P}. \tag{2}
\]

We now compute the required quantities in terms of \(u,v\).

First,
\[
\tfrac12-x_P = \frac12 - \frac{1-uv}{2D} = \frac{D-(1-uv)}{2D}.
\]
But it is easier to use the earlier expression we derived: \( \frac12-x_P = \frac{u-v}{2D}\).  Let's verify:
\(D = (1+u)(1-v) = 1 - v + u - uv\).  Then
\[
\frac12-x_P = \frac12 - \frac{1-uv}{2D} = \frac{D - (1-uv)}{2D} = \frac{ (1-u)(1-v) - (1-uv) }{2D} = \frac{ (1-u)(1-v) - 1 + uv }{2D}.
\]
Now \((1-u)(1-v) = 1 - u - v + uv\).  So numerator becomes \(1 - u - v + uv - 1 + uv = -u - v + 2uv\).  That's not obviously \(\frac{u-v}{2D}\).  Let's recompute using our earlier derived formula: \(1/2 - x_P = \frac{u - v}{2(1+u)(1-v)}\).  We derived that from the expression for \(c_y d_y\).  Let's check with numeric: \(u=0.2,v=0.4\) gave \(1/2 - x_P = -5/36\), and \(\frac{u-v}{2D} = \frac{-0.2}{2*0.72} = -0.2/1.44 = -0.13889\), which is \(-5/36\).  So indeed \(\frac12-x_P = \frac{u-v}{2D}\).  So we accept that.

Thus
\[
\frac12-x_P = \frac{u-v}{2D}. \tag{3}
\]

Now compute \((y_P-m)(n-y_P)\).

We have \(y_P = \frac{u+v}{2D}\).  Also,
\[
m = \frac{u}{1-u^2} = \frac{u}{(1-u)(1+u)}.
\]
From earlier we derived a simplification:
\[
y_P-m = \frac{n-m + r_2 - r_1}{2} \quad\text{and}\quad n-y_P = \frac{n-m - r_2 + r_1}{2},
\]
but perhaps we can compute directly using the expressions for \(y_P\), \(m\), \(n\).  Instead, we use the identity we discovered:
\[
(y_P-m)(n-y_P) = \frac{(v-u)^2}{4(1-u^2)(1-v^2)}.
\]
Let's verify that this matches our earlier derivation.  We had:
\((y_P-m)(n-y_P) = \frac{(n-m)^2 - (r_2-r_1)^2}{4}\).  Then we expressed \(n-m\) and \(r_2-r_1\) in terms of \(u,v\) and obtained:
\[
\frac{(v-u)^2}{4(1-u^2)(1-v^2)}.
\]
Indeed, we derived that after simplification.  So we can use that.

Thus
\[
(y_P-m)(n-y_P) = \frac{(v-u)^2}{4(1-u^2)(1-v^2)}. \tag{4}
\]

Now compute \(h\) using (2) and (3):
\[
h = \frac12 - \frac{ \frac{(v-u)^2}{4(1-u^2)(1-v^2)} }{ \frac{u-v}{2D} } = \frac12 - \frac{(v-u)^2}{4(1-u^2)(1-v^2)} \cdot \frac{2D}{u-v}.
\]
Since \(v-u = -(u-v)\), we have \((v-u)^2 = (u-v)^2\).  So
\[
h = \frac12 - \frac{(u-v)^2}{4(1-u^2)(1-v^2)} \cdot \frac{2D}{u-v} = \frac12 - \frac{(u-v)}{2(1-u^2)(1-v^2)} \cdot D.
\]
Now \(D = (1+u)(1-v)\).  Also,
\[
\frac{1}{(1-u^2)(1-v^2)} = \frac{1}{(1-u)(1+u)(1-v)(1+v)}.
\]
Thus
\[
h = \frac12 - \frac{(u-v)}{2} \cdot \frac{D}{(1-u^2)(1-v^2)}.
\]

But perhaps we can simplify further using the expression we derived earlier: \(h = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1+v)(1-u)}\).  Let's see if that matches.  Our current expression is:
\[
h = \frac12 - \frac{(u-v)}{2} \cdot \frac{D}{(1-u^2)(1-v^2)}.
\]
Plug \(D = (1+u)(1-v)\).  Then
\[
\frac{D}{(1-u^2)(1-v^2)} = \frac{(1+u)(1-v)}{(1-u)(1+u)(1-v)(1+v)} = \frac{1}{(1-u)(1+v)}.
\]
Thus
\[
h = \frac12 - \frac{(u-v)}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{(1-u)(1+v)} = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}.
\]
But \(u-v = -(v-u)\).  So
\[
h = \frac12 + \frac{v-u}{2(1-u)(1+v)}.
\]
That's a different sign than before.  However earlier we had \(R = \frac{u-v}{2(1+v)(1-u)}\) and then \(h = \frac12 - R\).  That gives \(h = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1+v)(1-u)}\).  That's the same as \(\frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}\) (since denominator is commutative).  So \(h = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}\).  Our derived expression is \(\frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}\)?  Let's compare: we got \(h = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}\).  That's exactly the same because we had \(h = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}\) (since we got \(h = \frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)}\)).  So it's consistent.

Now we have \(h\) and \(y_P\).  So
\[
H = \Bigl(\frac12 - \frac{u-v}{2(1-u)(1+v)},\; \frac{u+v}{2(1+u)(1-v)}\Bigr). \tag{5}
\]

---

### Points \(E\) and \(F\)

The line \(AP\) is the line through \(A=(0,0)\) and \(P\).  Therefore points on \(AP\) are scalar multiples of \(P\).  The second intersection of this line with \(\Omega\) (circle centred at \(M\) with radius \(r_1\)) is obtained by solving \(|tP-M|^2 = r_1^2\) with \(t\neq0\).  Since \(A\) lies on \(\Omega\), we have \(|M|^2 = r_1^2\).  Expanding:
\[
t^2|P|^2 - 2t\,(M\cdot P) + |M|^2 = r_1^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; t^2|P|^2 - 2t\,(M\cdot P) = 0.
\]
Thus \(t=0\) or \(t = \frac{2\,(M\cdot P)}{|P|^2}\).  Hence
\[
E = \frac{2\,(M\cdot P)}{|P|^2}\,P.
\]
Similarly,
\[
F = \frac{2\,(N\cdot P)}{|P|^2}\,P.
\]

We now compute \(M\cdot P\) and \(N\cdot P\) in terms of \(u,v\).  From the expressions for \(P\) and \(M\) we have
\[
M\cdot P = \frac12 x_P + m y_P.
\]
We already computed earlier:
\[
M\cdot P = \frac{1+u^2+uv+u^3v}{4D(1-u^2)}.
\]
But we can also obtain a simpler rational expression after cancellation with \(|P|^2\).  Let's compute \(\alpha = \frac{2\,(M\cdot P)}{|P|^2}\) and \(\beta\) similarly.  We'll find that
\[
\alpha = \frac{81}{58},\quad \beta = \frac{162}{91}
\]
when \(u=1/5,\;v=2/5\).  In general they are rational functions.

However we don't need the explicit forms of \(\alpha,\beta\) for the tangency proof if we use the linear equations for the circumcenter of \(BEF\).  Let's proceed.

---

### Circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\)

Let \(v = P\).  Then \(E = \alpha v\), \(F = \beta v\), \(B = (1,0)\).  The circumcenter \(O\) of \(\triangle BEF\) satisfies
\[
O\cdot v = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2}\,|v|^2, \qquad O\cdot (v - B) = \frac{\alpha^2|v|^2 - 1}{2}. \tag{6}
\]
(This follows from \(|O-B|^2 = |O-\alpha v|^2\) and \(|O-\alpha v|^2 = |O-\beta v|^2\).)  Solving the linear system for \(O\) gives
\[
O_x = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2}\,|v|^2 - \frac{\alpha^2|v|^2 - 1}{2}, \qquad O_y = \frac{ \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2}\,|v|^2 - a O_x }{b}. \tag{7}
\]

Now we need to prove that the line through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\) (i.e. parallel to \(v\)) is tangent to the circle \((BEF)\).  The line \(L\) through \(H\) with direction \(v\) has equation \(z = H + t v\) (\(t\in\mathbb R\)).  Its distance from \(O\) is
\[
\frac{|\det(O-H,\;v)|}{|v|}.
\]
The circle \((BEF)\) is tangent to \(L\) iff
\[
\frac{|\det(O-H,\;v)|}{|v|} = |O-B|. \tag{8}
\]

We will verify that (8) holds identically in \(u,v\) (for all admissible values).  The computation is heavy but simplifies dramatically when we use the expressions we derived for \(P\) and \(H\) and the values of \(\alpha,\beta\) in terms of \(u,v\).

To avoid excessive algebra we can instead use the following strategy: Show that \(O\) lies on the line that is the reflection of the line \(PH\) across the line that is the angle bisector of something.  But maybe the algebraic route is simpler after all if we carefully compute.

We can compute \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\) explicitly in terms of \(u,v\).  Let's do that.

We have:
\[
M\cdot P = \frac12 a + m b.
\]
With \(a = \frac{1-uv}{2D},\; b = \frac{u+v}{2D},\; m = \frac{u}{1-u^2}\).

We derived earlier:
\[
M\cdot P = \frac{1+u^2+uv+u^3v}{4D(1-u^2)}.
\]
But perhaps we can simplify by noting that \(|P|^2 = \frac{1+u^2v^2+u^2+v^2}{4D^2}\).  Then
\[
\alpha = \frac{2\,(M\cdot P)}{|P|^2} = \frac{2\cdot \frac{1+u^2+uv+u^3v}{4D(1-u^2)}}{\frac{1+u^2v^2+u^2+v^2}{4D^2}} = \frac{1+u^2+uv+u^3v}{D(1-u^2)} \cdot \frac{D^2}{1+u^2v^2+u^2+v^2} = \frac{(1+u^2+uv+u^3v) D}{(1-u^2)(1+u^2v^2+u^2+v^2)}.
\]

Similarly,
\[
\beta = \frac{(1+uv+v^2+u v^3) D}{(1-v^2)(1+u^2v^2+u^2+v^2)}.
\]

Now this is messy.  However we can simplify by noticing that the expressions \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\) appear in the combination \(\frac{\alpha+\beta}{2}\,|v|^2\) and \(\alpha^2|v|^2\) etc.  But maybe we can find a simpler representation by using the half-angle parameters we already introduced.  In fact, we can also express \(M\cdot P\) in terms of \(u,v\) more elegantly using the formulas we derived for \(c_y\) and \(d_y\) and the relation between \(P\) and \(M,N\).  Let's try a different approach.

Recall that we have the coordinates of \(P\) in terms of \(u,v\).  Also, we have the coordinates of \(M\) and \(N\) in terms of \(u,v\).  Perhaps we can compute the circumcenter \(O\) of \(BEF\) using the fact that it is the midpoint of the arc \(EF\) of the circle that passes through \(A\) and \(B\)?  Not.

Another observation: The points \(E\) and \(F\) are the second intersections of the line \(AP\) with \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\).  The circle \((BEF)\) is called the "Miquel circle" of quadrilateral?  Actually consider quadrilateral \(M N A B\)?  Not.

Let's try to derive an equation for the circle \((BEF)\) directly using coordinates.  We have \(B=(1,0)\), \(E=\alpha (a,b)\), \(F=\beta (a,b)\).  The circumcircle can be described by the equation
\[
\begin{vmatrix}
x^2+y^2 & x & y & 1 \\
1 & 1 & 0 & 1 \\
\alpha^2|v|^2 & \alpha a & \alpha b & 1 \\
\beta^2|v|^2 & \beta a & \beta b & 1
\end{vmatrix} = 0.
\]
But maybe we can find a simpler characterization: Because \(E\) and \(F\) are collinear with the origin, the circle through \(B\) and those two points is coaxial with the circle with diameter \(EF\)?  Actually, for any three points, the circumcenter is given by the intersection of perpendicular bisectors.  Since we have an expression for \(O\) in terms of \(\alpha,\beta,a,b\), we can attempt to compute \(O\) symbolically.  But perhaps we can avoid computing \(O\) explicitly by using the following idea: The line \(L\) is parallel to \(AP\).  If we reflect \(H\) across the line that is the angle bisector of something, we might get a point on \((BEF)\).  However, maybe there is a more clever analytic approach: Use vectors and treat the problem as proving that \(H\) is the orthocenter of triangle \(PMN\) and then consider the homothety centered at \(A\) that sends the circle \((ACD)\) to the circle \((BEF)\).  Let's explore that.

We have triangle \(ACD\) and its circumcenter \(P\).  The line \(AP\)
